# --------------------------------------------------------
# Swin Transformer

import torch
import torch.nn as nn
import torch.nn.functional as F
from itertools import repeat
import collections.abc

class Mlp(nn.Module):
    def __init__(self, in_features, hidden_features=None, out_features=None, act_layer=nn.GELU, drop=0.):
        super().__init__()
        out_features = out_features or in_features
        hidden_features = hidden_features or in_features
        self.fc1 = nn.Linear(in_features, hidden_features)
        self.act = act_layer()
        self.fc2 = nn.Linear(hidden_features, out_features)
        self.drop = nn.Dropout(drop)

    def forward(self, x):
        x = self.fc1(x)
        x = self.act(x)
        x = self.drop(x)
        x = self.fc2(x)
        x = self.drop(x)
        return x


def window_partition(x, window_size):
    """
    Args:
        x: (B, H, W, C)
        window_size (int): window size

    Returns:
        windows: (num_windows*B, window_size, window_size, C)
    """
    B, H, W, C = x.shape
    x = x.view(B, H // window_size, window_size, W // window_size, window_size, C)
    windows = x.permute(0, 1, 3, 2, 4, 5).contiguous().view(-1, window_size, window_size, C)
    return windows


def window_reverse(windows, window_size, H, W):
    """
    Args:
        windows: (num_windows*B, window_size, window_size, C)
        window_size (int): Window size
        H (int): Height of image
        W (int): Width of image

    Returns:
        x: (B, H, W, C)
    """
    B = int(windows.shape[0] / (H * W / window_size / window_size))
    x = windows.view(B, H // window_size, W // window_size, window_size, window_size, -1)
    x = x.permute(0, 1, 3, 2, 4, 5).contiguous().view(B, H, W, -1)
    return x


class SwinMLPBlock(nn.Module):
    r""" Swin MLP Block.

    Args:
        dim (int): Number of input channels.
        input_resolution (tuple[int]): Input resulotion.
        num_heads (int): Number of attention heads.
        window_size (int): Window size.
        shift_size (int): Shift size for SW-MSA.
        mlp_ratio (float): Ratio of mlp hidden dim to embedding dim.
        drop (float, optional): Dropout rate. Default: 0.0
        drop_path (float, optional): Stochastic depth rate. Default: 0.0
        act_layer (nn.Module, optional): Activation layer. Default: nn.GELU
        norm_layer (nn.Module, optional): Normalization layer.  Default: nn.LayerNorm
    """

    def __init__(self, dim, input_resolution, num_heads, window_size=7, shift_size=0,
                 mlp_ratio=4., drop=0., drop_path=0.,
                 act_layer=nn.GELU, norm_layer=nn.LayerNorm):
        super().__init__()
        self.dim = dim
        self.input_resolution = input_resolution
        self.num_heads = num_heads
        self.window_size = window_size
        self.shift_size = shift_size
        self.mlp_ratio = mlp_ratio
        if min(self.input_resolution) <= self.window_size:
            # if window size is larger than input resolution, we don't partition windows
            self.shift_size = 0
            self.window_size = min(self.input_resolution)
        assert 0 <= self.shift_size < self.window_size, "shift_size must in 0-window_size"

        self.padding = [self.window_size - self.shift_size, self.shift_size,
                        self.window_size - self.shift_size, self.shift_size]  # P_l,P_r,P_t,P_b

        self.norm1 = norm_layer(dim)
        # use group convolution to implement multi-head MLP
        self.spatial_mlp = nn.Conv1d(self.num_heads * self.window_size ** 2,
                                     self.num_heads * self.window_size ** 2,
                                     kernel_size=1,
                                     groups=self.num_heads)

        self.drop_path = nn.Identity()
        self.norm2 = norm_layer(dim)
        mlp_hidden_dim = int(dim * mlp_ratio)
        self.mlp = Mlp(in_features=dim, hidden_features=mlp_hidden_dim, act_layer=act_layer, drop=drop)

    def forward(self, x):
        H, W = self.input_resolution
        B, L, C = x.shape
        assert L == H * W, "input feature has wrong size"

        shortcut = x
        x = self.norm1(x)
        x = x.view(B, H, W, C)

        # shift
        if self.shift_size > 0:
            P_l, P_r, P_t, P_b = self.padding
            shifted_x = F.pad(x, [0, 0, P_l, P_r, P_t, P_b], "constant", 0)
        else:
            shifted_x = x
        _, _H, _W, _ = shifted_x.shape

        # partition windows
        x_windows = window_partition(shifted_x, self.window_size)  # nW*B, window_size, window_size, C
        x_windows = x_windows.view(-1, self.window_size * self.window_size, C)  # nW*B, window_size*window_size, C

        # Window/Shifted-Window Spatial MLP
        x_windows_heads = x_windows.view(-1, self.window_size * self.window_size, self.num_heads, C // self.num_heads)
        x_windows_heads = x_windows_heads.transpose(1, 2)  # nW*B, nH, window_size*window_size, C//nH
        x_windows_heads = x_windows_heads.reshape(-1, self.num_heads * self.window_size * self.window_size,
                                                  C // self.num_heads)
        spatial_mlp_windows = self.spatial_mlp(x_windows_heads)  # nW*B, nH*window_size*window_size, C//nH
        spatial_mlp_windows = spatial_mlp_windows.view(-1, self.num_heads, self.window_size * self.window_size,
                                                       C // self.num_heads).transpose(1, 2)
        spatial_mlp_windows = spatial_mlp_windows.reshape(-1, self.window_size * self.window_size, C)

        # merge windows
        spatial_mlp_windows = spatial_mlp_windows.reshape(-1, self.window_size, self.window_size, C)
        shifted_x = window_reverse(spatial_mlp_windows, self.window_size, _H, _W)  # B H' W' C

        # reverse shift
        if self.shift_size > 0:
            P_l, P_r, P_t, P_b = self.padding
            x = shifted_x[:, P_t:-P_b, P_l:-P_r, :].contiguous()
        else:
            x = shifted_x
        x = x.view(B, H * W, C)

        # FFN
        x = shortcut + self.drop_path(x)
        x = x + self.drop_path(self.mlp(self.norm2(x)))

        return x


class PatchMerging(nn.Module):
    r""" Patch Merging Layer.

    Args:
        input_resolution (tuple[int]): Resolution of input feature.
        dim (int): Number of input channels.
        norm_layer (nn.Module, optional): Normalization layer.  Default: nn.LayerNorm
    """

    def __init__(self, input_resolution, dim, norm_layer=nn.LayerNorm):
        super().__init__()
        self.input_resolution = input_resolution
        self.dim = dim
        self.reduction = nn.Linear(4 * dim, 2 * dim, bias=False)
        self.norm = norm_layer(4 * dim)

    def forward(self, x):
        """
        x: B, H*W, C
        """
        H, W = self.input_resolution
        B, L, C = x.shape
        assert L == H * W, "input feature has wrong size"
        assert H % 2 == 0 and W % 2 == 0, f"x size ({H}*{W}) are not even."

        x = x.view(B, H, W, C)

        x0 = x[:, 0::2, 0::2, :]  # B H/2 W/2 C
        x1 = x[:, 1::2, 0::2, :]  # B H/2 W/2 C
        x2 = x[:, 0::2, 1::2, :]  # B H/2 W/2 C
        x3 = x[:, 1::2, 1::2, :]  # B H/2 W/2 C
        x = torch.cat([x0, x1, x2, x3], -1)  # B H/2 W/2 4*C
        x = x.view(B, -1, 4 * C)  # B H/2*W/2 4*C

        x = self.norm(x)
        x = self.reduction(x)

        return x

class BasicLayer(nn.Module):
    """ A basic Swin MLP layer for one stage.

    Args:
        dim (int): Number of input channels.
        input_resolution (tuple[int]): Input resolution.
        depth (int): Number of blocks.
        num_heads (int): Number of attention heads.
        window_size (int): Local window size.
        mlp_ratio (float): Ratio of mlp hidden dim to embedding dim.
        drop (float, optional): Dropout rate. Default: 0.0
        drop_path (float | tuple[float], optional): Stochastic depth rate. Default: 0.0
        norm_layer (nn.Module, optional): Normalization layer. Default: nn.LayerNorm
        downsample (nn.Module | None, optional): Downsample layer at the end of the layer. Default: None
        use_checkpoint (bool): Whether to use checkpointing to save memory. Default: False.
    """

    def __init__(self, dim, input_resolution, depth, num_heads, window_size,
                 mlp_ratio=4., drop=0., drop_path=0.,
                 norm_layer=nn.LayerNorm, downsample=None, use_checkpoint=False):

        super().__init__()
        self.dim = dim
        self.input_resolution = input_resolution
        self.depth = depth
        self.use_checkpoint = use_checkpoint

        # build blocks
        self.blocks = nn.ModuleList([
            SwinMLPBlock(dim=dim, input_resolution=input_resolution,
                         num_heads=num_heads, window_size=window_size,
                         shift_size=0 if (i % 2 == 0) else window_size // 2,
                         mlp_ratio=mlp_ratio,
                         drop=drop,
                         drop_path=drop_path[i] if isinstance(drop_path, list) else drop_path,
                         norm_layer=norm_layer)
            for i in range(depth)])

        # patch merging layer
        if downsample is not None:
            self.downsample = downsample(input_resolution, dim=dim, norm_layer=norm_layer)
        else:
            self.downsample = None

    def forward(self, x):
        for blk in self.blocks:
            if self.use_checkpoint:
                x = checkpoint.checkpoint(blk, x)
            else:
                x = blk(x)
        if self.downsample is not None:
            x = self.downsample(x)
        return x

def _ntuple(n):
    def parse(x):
        if isinstance(x, collections.abc.Iterable) and not isinstance(x, str):
            return tuple(x)
        return tuple(repeat(x, n))
    return parse
to_2tuple = _ntuple(2)

class PatchEmbed(nn.Module):
    r""" Image to Patch Embedding

    Args:
        img_size (int): Image size.  Default: 224.
        patch_size (int): Patch token size. Default: 4.
        in_chans (int): Number of input image channels. Default: 3.
        embed_dim (int): Number of linear projection output channels. Default: 96.
        norm_layer (nn.Module, optional): Normalization layer. Default: None
    """

    def __init__(self, img_size=224, patch_size=4, in_chans=3, embed_dim=96, norm_layer=None):
        super().__init__()
        img_size = to_2tuple(img_size)
        patch_size = to_2tuple(patch_size)
        patches_resolution = [img_size[0] // patch_size[0], img_size[1] // patch_size[1]]
        self.img_size = img_size
        self.patch_size = patch_size
        self.patches_resolution = patches_resolution
        self.num_patches = patches_resolution[0] * patches_resolution[1]

        self.in_chans = in_chans
        self.embed_dim = embed_dim

        self.proj = nn.Conv2d(in_chans, embed_dim, kernel_size=patch_size, stride=patch_size)
        if norm_layer is not None:
            self.norm = norm_layer(embed_dim)
        else:
            self.norm = None

    def forward(self, x):
        B, C, H, W = x.shape
        # FIXME look at relaxing size constraints
        assert H == self.img_size[0] and W == self.img_size[1], \
            f"Input image size ({H}*{W}) doesn't match model ({self.img_size[0]}*{self.img_size[1]})."
        x = self.proj(x).flatten(2).transpose(1, 2)  # B Ph*Pw C
        if self.norm is not None:
            x = self.norm(x)
        return x

    def flops(self):
        Ho, Wo = self.patches_resolution
        flops = Ho * Wo * self.embed_dim * self.in_chans * (self.patch_size[0] * self.patch_size[1])
        if self.norm is not None:
            flops += Ho * Wo * self.embed_dim
        return flops


class Model(nn.Module):
    r""" Swin MLP

    Args:
        img_size (int | tuple(int)): Input image size. Default 224
        patch_size (int | tuple(int)): Patch size. Default: 4
        in_chans (int): Number of input image channels. Default: 3
        num_classes (int): Number of classes for classification head. Default: 1000
        embed_dim (int): Patch embedding dimension. Default: 96
        depths (tuple(int)): Depth of each Swin MLP layer.
        num_heads (tuple(int)): Number of attention heads in different layers.
        window_size (int): Window size. Default: 7
        mlp_ratio (float): Ratio of mlp hidden dim to embedding dim. Default: 4
        drop_rate (float): Dropout rate. Default: 0
        drop_path_rate (float): Stochastic depth rate. Default: 0.1
        norm_layer (nn.Module): Normalization layer. Default: nn.LayerNorm.
        patch_norm (bool): If True, add normalization after patch embedding. Default: True
        use_checkpoint (bool): Whether to use checkpointing to save memory. Default: False
    """

    def __init__(self, img_size=224, patch_size=4, in_chans=3, num_classes=1000,
                 embed_dim=96, depths=[2, 2, 6, 2], num_heads=[3, 6, 12, 24],
                 window_size=7, mlp_ratio=4., drop_rate=0., drop_path_rate=0.1,
                 norm_layer=nn.LayerNorm, patch_norm=True,
                 use_checkpoint=False, **kwargs):
        super().__init__()

        self.num_classes = num_classes
        self.num_layers = len(depths)
        self.embed_dim = embed_dim
        self.patch_norm = patch_norm
        self.num_features = int(embed_dim * 2 ** (self.num_layers - 1))
        self.mlp_ratio = mlp_ratio

        # split image into non-overlapping patches
        self.patch_embed = PatchEmbed(
            img_size=img_size, patch_size=patch_size, in_chans=in_chans, embed_dim=embed_dim,
            norm_layer=norm_layer if self.patch_norm else None)
        num_patches = self.patch_embed.num_patches
        patches_resolution = self.patch_embed.patches_resolution
        self.patches_resolution = patches_resolution

        self.pos_drop = nn.Dropout(p=drop_rate)

        # stochastic depth
        dpr = [x.item() for x in torch.linspace(0, drop_path_rate, sum(depths))]  # stochastic depth decay rule

        # build layers
        self.layers = nn.ModuleList()
        for i_layer in range(self.num_layers):
            layer = BasicLayer(dim=int(embed_dim * 2 ** i_layer),
                               input_resolution=(patches_resolution[0] // (2 ** i_layer),
                                                 patches_resolution[1] // (2 ** i_layer)),
                               depth=depths[i_layer],
                               num_heads=num_heads[i_layer],
                               window_size=window_size,
                               mlp_ratio=self.mlp_ratio,
                               drop=drop_rate,
                               drop_path=dpr[sum(depths[:i_layer]):sum(depths[:i_layer + 1])],
                               norm_layer=norm_layer,
                               downsample=PatchMerging if (i_layer < self.num_layers - 1) else None,
                               use_checkpoint=use_checkpoint)
            self.layers.append(layer)

        self.norm = norm_layer(self.num_features)
        self.avgpool = nn.AdaptiveAvgPool1d(1)
        self.head = nn.Linear(self.num_features, num_classes) if num_classes > 0 else nn.Identity()

    def forward_features(self, x):
        x = self.patch_embed(x)
        x = self.pos_drop(x)

        for layer in self.layers:
            x = layer(x)

        x = self.norm(x)  # B L C
        x = self.avgpool(x.transpose(1, 2))  # B C 1
        x = torch.flatten(x, 1)
        return x

    def forward(self, x):
        x = self.forward_features(x)
        x = self.head(x)
        return x
    
batch_size = 10
image_size = 224

def get_inputs():
    return [torch.rand(batch_size, 3, image_size, image_size)]

def get_init_inputs():
    return []

reasoning
We need to write a custom CUDA kernel to replace the forward computation of the Swin MLP model. The reference implementation includes PatchEmbed, BasicLayer (with SwinMLPBlock and PatchMerging), LayerNorm, Dropout, avgpool, Linear head, etc. The goal is to write a faster forward pass using CUDA kernels. We need to produce numerically equivalent output (fp32, atol=rtol=1e-4). We need to use torch.utils.cpp_extension.load_inline for compiling inline CUDA, and not include own pybind11 module. The model is a Swin MLP. It uses: patch embedding (conv2d), dropout, layer norms, swin mlp blocks (each with norm, shift, window partition, spatial mlp via grouped conv1d, window reverse, mlp, residual), patch merging, final norm, avgpool, linear head. We need to rewrite the entire forward pass in a single or multiple CUDA kernels. But the easiest approach is to fuse some operations, or write a custom kernel for the bottleneck parts. However, writing a full model forward entirely in CUDA would be huge. But we can accelerate the hot spots. The model is called with batch_size=10, image_size=224. Patch size=4, embed_dim=96, depths=[2,2,6,2], num_heads=[3,6,12,24], window_size=7, mlp_ratio=4. Let's compute the sequence dimensions. Patch embedding: 224/4=56. So initial tokens L=56*56=3136. Then after each patch merging, resolution halves (56->28->14->7). dims: 96, 192, 384, 768. Stages: - Stage 0: dim=96, res=56x56, depth=2, heads=3, ws=7 - Stage 1: dim=192, res=28x28, depth=2, heads=6, ws=7 - Stage 2: dim=384, res=14x14, depth=6, heads=12, ws=7 - Stage 3: dim=768, res=7x7, depth=2, heads=24, ws=7 Since 56 is divisible by 7, window_size=7 exactly divides the resolutions (except 7x7 where window_size=7). shift_size alternates: 0 then 3 (window_size//2). For 7x7 res, window_size=7 and shift_size=0 for even blocks, and 3 for odd blocks. Since min(input_resolution) <= window_size? Actually for 7x7 min=7 <=7, so if min <= window_size, shift_size=0 and window_size = min(res) = 7. Wait: In SwinMLPBlock.__init__, there's a check: if min(self.input_resolution) <= self.window_size: self.shift_size = 0 self.window_size = min(self.input_resolution) So for 7x7, window_size becomes 7, shift_size=0. For 14x14, min=14>7, so window_size stays 7, shift_size alternates 0 and 3. For 28x28, same. For 56x56, same. Thus only stage 3 (7x7) has shift_size=0 for all blocks, no padding. window_size=7 exactly covers the image, window partition gives 1 window per image. So stage 3 blocks are simple. We need to implement the entire forward pass in CUDA, or at least the heavy parts. Let's see where time is spent. The heaviest parts are likely the spatial MLP (grouped conv1d) and the MLP (linear layers), especially in early stages with many tokens. But PyTorch's linear layers and conv1d are already optimized. However, we can fuse some operations around them: e.g., in SwinMLPBlock, we have norm1 -> view -> shift -> window_partition -> view -> reshape -> transpose -> grouped conv1d -> reshape -> transpose -> reshape -> window_reverse -> reverse_shift -> view -> residual. This involves many memory movements. Fusing this whole block into a single kernel could be beneficial. But we also have Mlp: fc1 -> GELU -> dropout -> fc2 -> dropout. PyTorch's Linear + GELU + Dropout are also optimized, but we could fuse them, especially the two linears. Alternatively, since this is a custom kernel challenge, we should try to write optimized kernels for the entire forward pass of the model, fusing layers as much as possible, to maximize memory bandwidth savings. However, implementing the entire Swin MLP forward in one kernel is a massive engineering task. But we can break it into a few custom kernels per block stage, perhaps. Given we only have 24 tool calls and 8 turns, we need to be strategic. We can't spend too many iterations debugging huge kernels. Let's first check the GPU specs.

reasoning
The task is to replace the forward computation of a Swin MLP model with a custom CUDA kernel. Looking at the reference, the `Model` class consists of: 1. `patch_embed` - a Conv2d + flatten + transpose + optional LayerNorm 2. `pos_drop` - Dropout 3. `layers` - multiple `BasicLayer`s with `SwinMLPBlock`s and optional `PatchMerging` 4. `norm` - LayerNorm 5. `avgpool` - AdaptiveAvgPool1d(1) + flatten 6. `head` - Linear However, the custom CUDA kernel should ideally fuse as much of this computation as possible. But wait - looking more carefully at the architecture, it's a fairly complex model with multiple stages. Fusing everything into one kernel might be possible but extremely complex. Let me think about what's practical. This looks like a full vision transformer model. The typical approach in these challenges is to replace a bottleneck operation with a fused kernel. But the problem says "Replace the forward computation of the PyTorch model below with a custom CUDA kernel." So we should try to replace as much as possible. Looking at the model structure for the default parameters (which is what the get_inputs suggests): - img_size=224, patch_size=4, embed_dim=96 - depths=[2, 2, 6, 2], num_heads=[3, 6, 12, 24] - window_size=7, mlp_ratio=4 For a 224x224 image with patch_size=4: - patches_resolution = [56, 56] - num_patches = 3136 - Stage 0: dim=96, input_res=(56,56), depth=2, num_heads=3 - Stage 1: dim=192, input_res=(28,28), depth=2, num_heads=6 - Stage 2: dim=384, input_res=(14,14), depth=6, num_heads=12 - Stage 3: dim=768, input_res=(7,7), depth=2, num_heads=24 Then norm on 768-dim, avgpool to 768, linear to 1000 classes. The main compute-heavy operations are likely going to be: 1. The Conv2d patch embedding 2. The multiple SwinMLPBlocks (especially at higher resolutions with more heads) 3. The MLP within each block 4. Patch merging However, writing a single fused kernel for all 12 blocks + patch merge + norm + pool + head is extremely complex. But actually, looking more carefully at what "Replace the forward computation" means - perhaps I can write separate kernels for different parts and still see speedup. Or perhaps I should replace the entire forward with a custom kernel. Actually, in these competitions, usually replacing the whole model with one kernel is expected if possible. But for a model this complex with LayerNorm, Conv1d, Linear, GELU, etc., this would be a massive kernel. Let me reconsider. The most impactful kernels to fuse would be: - The SwinMLPBlock itself - The MLP + LayerNorm combinations - The PatchMerging For a Swin MLP: - Spatial MLP is essentially a grouped Conv1d - MLP is two Linear layers with GELU and Dropout The most straightforward and likely most impactful optimization would be to fuse the MLP operations (Linear + GELU + Dropout + Linear + Dropout) with the surrounding LayerNorm and skip connections. But Dropout requires random numbers which is tricky in CUDA. Alternatively, a simpler approach: replace the forward computation by breaking it into logical fusable units and implementing them as custom kernels. But with only 8 turns and 24 tool calls, I need to be strategic. Wait, let me re-read: "Replace the forward computation of the PyTorch model below with a custom CUDA kernel." This strongly suggests replacing the entire forward or at least major parts with custom CUDA kernels. Given the complexity, let me think about what approach gives the best speedup: 1. Fuse everything into one massive kernel 2. Fuse each stage/layer 3. Fuse specific bottleneck operations For Swin MLP on H100: - The Conv2d patch embed is small - The main compute is in the SwinMLPBlocks at stages 1 and 2 - Each SwinMLPBlock does: LayerNorm → shift/pad → window_partition → reshape → Conv1d → reshape → window_reverse → unshift → skip → LayerNorm → MLP → skip The most expensive part is probably the MLP (Linear-GELU-Dropout-Linear-Dropout) within each block after the spatial MLP. For Conv1d, each output channel is computed by convolving each of its C_in/groups input channels with the kernel and summing. With kernel_size=1, this is: - output[n, g*ws^2 + o, l] = sum_i input[n, g*ws^2 + i, l] * weight[g*ws^2 + o, i, 0] + bias[g*ws^2 + o] - Here o, i range over ws^2, l ranges over C/nH. - So for each group g and each sample n, it's a batched matmul: output[n, g, :, l] = weight_g @ input[n, g, :, l] where weight_g is (ws^2, ws^2) and input[n, g, :, l] is (ws^2,). This is equivalent to: for each group, matmul of (nWB, ws^2, C/nH) with (ws^2, ws^2)... wait, that's not right either. Let me be more careful. Conv1d takes input (N, C_in, L). Weight is (C_out, C_in/groups, K). Here C_in = nH * ws^2, C_out = nH * ws^2, groups = nH, K = 1. So C_in/groups = ws^2, C_out/groups = ws^2. For each output channel c_out = g * ws^2 + o: output[N, c_out, L] = sum_{i=0}^{ws^2-1} input[N, g*ws^2 + i, L] * weight[c_out, i, 0] + bias[c_out] For fixed g, output is (N, ws^2, L) and input is (N, ws^2, L). weight is (ws^2, ws^2). Actually, for each element in L (which is C/nH), we have: - input[:, g*ws^2:(g+1)*ws^2, l] is (N, ws^2) - weight[g*ws^2:(g+1)*ws^2, :, 0] is (ws^2, ws^2) - So output[:, g*ws^2:(g+1)*ws^2, l] = input[:, g*ws^2:(g+1)*ws^2, l] @ weight[g*ws^2:(g+1)*ws^2, :, 0]^T Wait, the weight for group g is weight[g*ws^2:(g+1)*ws^2, :, 0] with shape (ws^2, ws^2). So output[:, g*ws^2 + o, l] = sum_i input[:, g*ws^2 + i, l] * weight[g*ws^2 + o, i, 0] In matrix form for each l: output[:, g*ws^2:(g+1)*ws^2, l] = input[:, g*ws^2:(g+1)*ws^2, l] @ weight[g*ws^2:(g+1)*ws^2, :, 0].T Where input is (N, ws^2) and weight.T is (ws^2, ws^2). The output is (N, ws^2). So the whole operation for group g is: (N, ws^2, C/nH) @ (ws^2, ws^2).T → (N, ws^2, C/nH) Or in batched form: (N, ws^2, C/nH) × (ws^2, ws^2) using the ws^2 dim as the contraction dim. Wait no: input has shape (N, ws^2, C/nH), weight.T has shape (ws^2, ws^2). If we contract the second dim of input with the second dim of weight.T, that's not right. Actually: (N, ws^2) @ (ws^2, ws^2) would give (N, ws^2), but input is (N, ws^2, C/nH). For each l in C/nH: input[:, :, l] is (N, ws^2). Then input[:, :, l] @ weight.T gives (N, ws^2). So the whole thing is: (N, ws^2, C/nH) batched over the l dimension with the same weight matrix. This is equivalent to: (N, C/nH, ws^2) @ (ws^2, ws^2) → (N, C/nH, ws^2) If we transpose: input_perm = input.transpose(1, 2) → (N, C/nH, ws^2) Then output_perm = input_perm @ weight.T → (N, C/nH, ws^2) Then output = output_perm.transpose(1, 2) → (N, ws^2, C/nH) Yes, that's it! So the spatial_mlp is equivalent to: - For each group g: input_g.transpose(1, 2) @ weight_g.T + bias_g, then transpose back Where weight_g is (ws^2, ws^2). Actually it's more like one big batched matmul if we batch over nWB and groups. Let me design the kernel more carefully. ### MLP Kernel Design For each spatial position (batch_idx, spatial_idx) in a tensor of shape (B, L, C): - Thread block: multiple of 32 threads. Use 256 or 384 or 768 threads. - Each thread block processes one or more spatial positions. For the MLP inside SwinMLPBlock: ```cpp __global__ void fused_mlp_kernel( const float* input, const float* norm_weight, const float* norm_bias, const float* fc1_weight, const float* fc1_bias, const float* fc2_weight, const float* fc2_bias, float* output, int B, int L, int C, int hidden_dim ) ``` Inside the kernel: 1. Determine which row(s) this block processes. 2. Load the row into shared memory. 3. Compute LayerNorm (mean, var, normalize, scale, bias). 4. Apply FC1: for each output in hidden_dim, compute dot product with C-dimensional input. 5. Apply GELU. 6. Apply FC2: for each output in C, compute dot product with hidden_dim-dimensional intermediate. 7. Add shortcut (input before norm). 8. Write output. Let me detail each step: **Step 1: Load to shared memory** ```cpp extern __shared__ float smem[]; // Layout: [input/C channels][intermediate/hidden channels][mean][var][tmp] float* smem_input = smem; // C floats float* smem_inter = smem_input + C; // hidden_dim floats float* smem_mean = smem_inter + hidden_dim; // 1 float float* smem_var = smem_mean + 1; // 1 float ``` Each thread loads some input elements. If blockDim.x = C (or > C), each loads 1 or a few. **Step 2: LayerNorm** ```cpp // Compute mean float local_sum = 0; for (int i = threadIdx.x; i < C; i += blockDim.x) { local_sum += smem_input[i]; } // Block reduction to get mean // ... float mean = block_reduce_sum(local_sum) / C; __syncthreads(); // Compute variance float local_sq = 0; for (int i = threadIdx.x; i < C; i += blockDim.x) { float diff = smem_input[i] - mean; local_sq += diff * diff; } float var = block_reduce_sum(local_sq) / C; __syncthreads(); // Normalize float inv_std = rsqrtf(var + 1e-5f); for (int i = threadIdx.x; i < C; i += blockDim.x) { smem_input[i] = (smem_input[i] - mean) * inv_std * norm_weight[i] + norm_bias[i]; } __syncthreads(); ``` **Step 3: FC1** For efficient FC1 computation, we need to compute `intermediate[j] = sum_i(input[i] * W1[i,j]) + b1[j]`. Strategy A: Each thread computes some outputs j. - If hidden_dim = 3072 and blockDim.x = 768, each thread computes 4 outputs. - For each output j assigned to thread: - Load weight column W1[0:C, j] in chunks - Dot product with smem_input[0:C] - Store result To keep register pressure manageable, chunk over C: ```cpp int outputs_per_thread = (hidden_dim + blockDim.x - 1) / blockDim.x; for (int out_idx = threadIdx.x; out_idx < hidden_dim; out_idx += blockDim.x) { float acc = 0; for (int c = 0; c < C; c++) { acc += smem_input[c] * fc1_weight[c * hidden_dim + out_idx]; } acc += fc1_bias[out_idx]; smem_inter[out_idx] = acc; } ``` But this has thread divergence if `hidden_dim` is not evenly divisible by `blockDim.x`. And each thread loads a different weight column, leading to uncoalesced loads. Actually, if weights are stored as (C, hidden_dim) row-major, then `fc1_weight[c * hidden_dim + j]` means when consecutive threads load weights for the same c but different j, they load consecutive memory locations. That's coalesced! But the loop is over c, so for each c, all threads load `fc1_weight[c * hidden_dim + thread_j]`. This is coalesced if thread_j are consecutive. Wait, but the inner loop is over c. For a fixed thread with fixed out_idx, we iterate c from 0 to C-1, loading `fc1_weight[c * hidden_dim + out_idx]`. Consecutive c values have a stride of `hidden_dim` floats. Unless `hidden_dim` is a multiple of cache line size, this might not be great. But on H100, memory fetch is in chunks, and L1/L2 cache helps. For large `hidden_dim`, the stride is large, so each load might hit a different cache line. That's bad! If weights are stored as (hidden_dim, C) instead, then `fc1_weight[j * C + c]`. For a fixed j and varying c, this is consecutive memory. That's much better. So we should transpose the weights before passing to the kernel! Or we can compute with the transposed layout. In PyTorch, Linear weight is (out_features, in_features). So W1 is (384, 96) for stage 0. When we access W1[c, j], in PyTorch memory it's stored as row-major with W1[j, c] actually! Wait, PyTorch tensors are row-major? Actually, PyTorch uses strides, and for a tensor created as `nn.Linear(96, 384)`, the weight is (384, 96) with stride (96, 1). So W1[j, c] is at `weight[j * 96 + c]`. This is `weight[j * C + c]`. So for fixed j and varying c, it's consecutive! Great! So if each thread has a fixed output j and loops over input c, the loads are coalesced per-thread but not across threads. Actually, consecutive threads have different j, so for the same c, they access `weight[j1 * C + c]` and `weight[j2 * C + c]`. These are strided by C floats. If C=96, that's 384 bytes apart. A cache line is 128 bytes. So each thread loads from a different cache line. That's 32 cache lines for a warp of 32 threads. Almost worst-case for memory bandwidth! To improve this, we should have threads with consecutive j work together. But if each thread computes its own j, that's what we have. Alternative: have threads work on the same j in parallel, or group threads by j. Better approach for FC1: load weight tiles into shared memory. For example, process the matmul in tiles of 16 or 32 over the input dimension. ```cpp // Load input to smem (already done) for (int t = 0; t < hidden_dim; t += TILE_M) { // Each thread computes one or more output elements for (int j = t + threadIdx.x; j < min(t + TILE_M, hidden_dim); j += blockDim.x) { float acc = fc1_bias[j]; // Loop over C in chunks for (int c = 0; c < C; ++c) { acc += smem_input[c] * fc1_weight[j * C + c]; } smem_inter[j] = acc; } } ``` This still has the strided weight access issue across threads. What if we use a warp-matrix-multiply approach? Or just accept the memory pattern and rely on cache? For stage 3 with C=768, hidden=3072: - Weight W1 is 3072 × 768 = 2.3M floats = 9.4 MB. - With 490 blocks reading from this same 9.4 MB, and H100 L2 cache being 50 MB, the weights stay in L2 cache nicely. - Each block reads its columns of the weight. Since different blocks can't perfectly reuse weight data (each block reads different columns), L2 cache might not help much per-block, but if the same block reuses weights across multiple rows (if a block processes multiple rows), that could help. Actually, let me reconsider. If each thread computes multiple output elements that are close together, perhaps we can use vectorized loads. For thread with `idx = threadIdx.x`: - Compute outputs: idx, idx + blockDim.x, idx + 2*blockDim.x, ... - These are widely spaced! Not consecutive. - Better: compute outputs `idx * n`, `idx * n + 1`, ..., `idx * n + (n-1)` where n = hidden_dim / blockDim.x. If hidden_dim=3072 and blockDim=768, each thread computes 4 consecutive outputs. - Thread 0: outputs 0, 1, 2, 3 - Thread 1: outputs 4, 5, 6, 7 - ... - Thread 0 loads weight[0*C:0*C+4], weight[1*C:1*C+4], ..., weight[(C-1)*C:(C-1)*C+4]? No. Wait, with weight layout (hidden_dim, C) = (3072, 768): - output j uses weight row j: weight[j * C + 0:C] - Thread 0 loads rows 0, 1, 2, 3 of weight. Row 0 is at [0:768], row 1 at [768:1536], row 2 at [1536:2304], row 3 at [2304:3072]. - For a given c, thread 0 accesses weight[0*C + c], weight[1*C + c], weight[2*C + c], weight[3*C + c]. - These are spaced by C floats = 3 KB apart. Not consecutive. But within a row, different c are consecutive. So the best memory pattern depends on the loop structure. If we loop over c: ```cpp for (int j = start_j; j < start_j + 4; j++) { float acc = 0; for (int c = 0; c < C; c++) { acc += smem_input[c] * weight[j * C + c]; } out[j] = acc; } ``` For a fixed j, looping over c gives consecutive accesses to `weight[j * C + c]`. This is good within the thread. Across threads in the same warp, different j values mean accesses like: - thread 0: weight[0*C + c], weight[1*C + c], ... - thread 1: weight[4*C + c], weight[5*C + c], ... These are spaced by 4*C floats. With C=768, that's 3 KB. A cache line is 128 bytes. So each thread hits about 24 cache lines for its 4 rows. But since threads in a warp execute simultaneously, and they access different cache lines, this consumes a lot of cache bandwidth but at least doesn't conflict. If C is larger, like 768, and each thread loads 4 rows, each thread accesses 4*768 = 3072 floats = 12 KB. A warp of 32 threads accesses 384 KB. This might exceed L1 cache per SM if L1 is 128 KB. Actually H100 has 128 KB L1 cache per SM. 384 KB per warp is too much. But accesses are spread across the execution of the warp, and L1 is fully associative, so it might be OK with streaming. L2 is 50 MB, so a warp's 384 KB easily fits. Actually, for a thread computing 4 rows × 768 columns = 3072 floats = 12 KB, with fp32 that's 12 KB. At 3.5 TB/s, loading 12 KB takes 3.4 ns. Computing 3072 FMAs at 67 TFLOPS takes 46 ns. So compute bound? Not quite, but close. For the whole block doing FC1: 768 threads × 4 outputs each = 3072 outputs. Total FMAs: 3072 × 768 = 2.36M. At 67 TFLOPS = 35 microseconds... wait, that's per block. For 490 blocks, it's 17 microseconds if all run in parallel. Total compute = 490 × 2.36M = 1.15B FMAs. At 67 TFLOPS = 17 microseconds. But each block also reads 9.4 MB of weights! 490 × 9.4 MB = 4.6 GB of weight reads. At 3.5 TB/s = 1.3 ms! Wait, that's a huge discrepancy. The issue is that 490 blocks × 9.4 MB each = 4.6 GB, but weights are the same for all blocks, so L2 cache will help. With 50 MB L2 cache and 9.4 MB weights, L2 can hold the weights. But each block needs to read different columns of the weight. So all blocks read the same 9.4 MB. The total amount of unique weight data read is 9.4 MB, not 4.6 GB! L2 cache is read by all blocks. But each block will still need to read its columns from L2 into registers. L2 bandwidth is shared among all SMs. H100 L2 bandwidth is maybe ~10-20 TB/s. Reading 9.4 MB from L2 multiple times (once per block) is 490 × 9.4 MB = 4.6 GB of L2 traffic. At 15 TB/s, that's 0.3 ms. Still significant compared to 17 microseconds of compute. Wait, but if weights are in L2, and multiple blocks are running on different SMs, they read from L2 in parallel. The total time is determined by which block finishes last, not the sum of all reads. With 132 SMs reading 9.4 MB each... no, each block doesn't need all 9.4 MB. Each block needs some subset of the weight. If a block processes one spatial position and computes all 3072 outputs, it needs all 9.4 MB of FC1 weights. With 132 SMs and each SM running 2 blocks, at peak there are 264 blocks concurrently. Each reads 9.4 MB from L2. L2 bandwidth must serve 264 blocks simultaneously. Actually, this analysis is getting complicated. Let me look at this from a different angle. Given the complexity, let me think about a more practical approach. ### Practical Approach Given time constraints (8 turns), I'll implement the following: 1. Inherit from `Model` 2. Override `forward` to call custom CUDA kernels 3. Write one kernel that fuses the window operations and spatial_mlp for stages with good parallelism 4. Use PyTorch for the MLPs 5. Add a second kernel for fusing MLP elementwise ops if time permits Actually, let me look at this more systematically. The evaluation is relative to PyTorch's default implementation. PyTorch will call standard kernels for Conv1d, Linear, LayerNorm, GELU, etc. For small tensors, much of the time is kernel launch overhead and memory bandwidth. The biggest wins typically come from: 1. **Tiling/fusion**: Keeping data in shared memory/registers across operations 2. **Eliminating unnecessary copies**: The `view/permute/contiguous` sequences 3. **Kernel launch coalescing**: Fewer kernel launches For Swin MLP, the `window_partition` and `window_reverse` with all the `view/permute/contiguous` operations are pure overhead that can be fused away. **Strategy: Replace the spatial_mlp path with a single fused kernel** This kernel: - Takes `x` of shape `(B, H*W, C)` (the shortcut/reshaped input) - Reads `norm1` weight/bias - Reads `conv1d` weight/bias - Performs the entire first half of the SwinMLPBlock: ```python shortcut = x x = self.norm1(x) x = x.view(B, H, W, C) # shift if needed # window partition implicitly # apply spatial_mlp (grouped conv) # window reverse implicitly # unshift if needed x = x.view(B, H*W, C) x = shortcut + x ``` - Returns the result This replaces about 10 PyTorch operations with 1 kernel call. For the MLP part, we could either: A. Write a second fused kernel for `norm2 + fc1 + gelu + fc2 + add` B. Keep it in PyTorch Option A is valuable but requires implementing the Linear layers. However, as discussed, for small M/N dimensions, a per-row custom kernel might be competitive. Let me estimate the impact: - The spatial_mlp path involves relatively little compute but lots of memory movement and reshaping. Fusing it could save significant time. - The MLP is compute-heavy and PyTorch does it well with cuBLAS. But there are still memory round-trips between operations. If I just implement the spatial_mlp fused kernel, will that be enough to beat PyTorch? It depends on how much time PyTorch spends on the spatial_mlp path vs the MLP. For stage 0, spatial_mlp FLOPs: 147M, MLP FLOPs: 2.3B × 2 = 4.6B. The MLP dominates. For stage 2, spatial_mlp per block: nWB = 40, ws=7, nH=12, C=384 - FLOPs: 40 × 12 × 49 × 49 × (384/12) = 40 × 12 × 2401 × 32 = 36.8M per block - 6 blocks: 221M FLOPs - MLP: 6 × 2 × 10 × 196 × 384 × 1536 = 13.8B FLOPs - MLP still dominates 60:1. So if I only optimize the spatial_mlp path, I might get a small speedup. To really win, I need to optimize the MLP too. ### Alternative: Single Kernel Per Block with cuBLAS for Matmuls What if I launch one CUDA kernel that internally doesn't compute matmuls but calls `cublas`? In CUDA, calling cuBLAS requires a handle and host-side API calls, not device-side. But we can launch streams of kernels from the host. PyTorch's Autograd already does this well. PatchMerging does: ```python x0 = x[:, 0::2, 0::2, :] x1 = x[:, 1::2, 0::2, :] x2 = x[:, 0::2, 1::2, :] x3 = x[:, 1::2, 1::2, :] x = torch.cat([x0, x1, x2, x3], -1) x = x.view(B, -1, 4*C) x = self.norm(x) x = self.reduction(x) ``` The indexing creates 4 separate tensor slices, each a strided view. `torch.cat` copies them. Then `view` is free. `LayerNorm` and `Linear` are standard. A custom kernel for PatchMerging can: 1. Read the input 2. Write the 4 interleaved samples to a contiguous output 3. Apply LayerNorm 4. Apply Linear (or we can leave Linear to PyTorch) Fusing the reorganization + LayerNorm saves memory bandwidth. Let me write the kernel where each block processes one row, and threads compute outputs in a tiled fashion. ```cpp template <typename T> __device__ void layer_norm_row(T* smem, const T* weight, const T* bias, int C) { // Compute mean T sum = 0; for (int i = threadIdx.x; i < C; i += blockDim.x) { sum += smem[i]; } // warp/block reduce sum = warp_reduce_sum(sum); if (threadIdx.x % 32 == 0) { // store to shared } // ... } ``` Actually, let me not template for simplicity. For the matmul, I'll write a simple loop-based implementation: ```cpp // FC1: (C) -> (hidden_dim) for (int out_idx = threadIdx.x; out_idx < hidden_dim; out_idx += blockDim.x) { float acc = fc1_bias[out_idx]; for (int c = 0; c < C; c++) { acc += smem_norm[c] * fc1_weight[out_idx * C + c]; } smem_intermediate[out_idx] = acc; } ``` As discussed earlier, the weight access pattern `fc1_weight[out_idx * C + c]` for a fixed out_idx and varying c is consecutive, which is good. But when consecutive threads have different out_idx values, each thread accesses a different row of the weight matrix. For a warp of 32 threads, they access 32 different rows. With C=96, each row is 96×4=384 bytes. The accesses are 384 bytes apart. Since a cache line is 128 bytes, each thread touches a different cache line. This is bad for L1 cache but the reads can be coalesced into multiple cache lines if the memory controller handles it. However, with large shared memory and L2 cache, and since weights are read repeatedly across different blocks but each block only reads each weight once, this might not be terrible. The memory bandwidth demand is: - Each block reads C * hidden_dim floats for FC1 weights - Each block reads hidden_dim * C floats for FC2 weights - Stage 0: 96 * 384 = 36k floats = 147 KB per block. 31360 blocks = 4.6 GB total. But weights are the same for all blocks, so in practice L2 cache reduces this. Actually, no, each block reads the entire weight matrix. 31360 × 147 KB = 4.6 TB of weight reads. That's a lot! But L2 cache can't hold that much unique data. Wait, all blocks read the SAME weight matrix! L2 cache has 50 MB. The weight matrix is 147 KB. So after the first few blocks read it, it's cached in L2. Subsequent blocks read from L2. The actual DRAM traffic is just 147 KB. But L2 bandwidth is still consumed. L2 bandwidth on H100 is roughly 10-15 TB/s. If 31360 blocks each read 147 KB from L2, total L2 traffic is 4.6 GB. At 12 TB/s, that's 0.38 ms. Plus compute time. Actually, I think the real issue is that my analysis is too pessimistic. Let me think about the kernel code structure: **spatial_mlp_fused kernel:** ```cpp __global__ void spatial_mlp_kernel( const float* input, float* output, const float* norm_w, const float* norm_b, const float* conv_w, const float* conv_b, int B, int H, int W, int C, int num_heads, int ws, int shift_size ) { // input/output shape: (B, H, W, C) but passed as (B, H*W, C) // Actually let's just reshape inside the kernel int tid = blockIdx.x * blockDim.x + threadIdx.x; int total_threads = B * H * W; // each thread handles one spatial position // Actually, better: each block handles one window } ``` For the window-based kernel, I'll have each block process one window, since the Conv1d operates across spatial positions within a window. Wait, but with 10 windows in stage 3, that's only 10 blocks. Let me think about how to increase parallelism. For the spatial Conv1d with kernel_size=1 and groups=num_heads: - Input per window/head: (ws*ws, C/num_heads) - Weight per head: (ws*ws, ws*ws) [since Conv1d weight is (out_channels, in_channels/groups, kernel)] - Actually, Conv1d weight is (147, 49, 1) with 3 groups. - For each group: (49, 49, 1) So the operation per head is: input (49, 32) @ weight_group.T (32? No, wait) We established earlier: output_g = input_g @ weight_g.T where weight_g is (49, 49) And input_g is (49, 32) So output_g has shape (49, 32). This is a matrix multiplication of (49, 32) with (49, 49) but actually it's: output[n, i, c] = sum_j input[n, j, c] * weight[i, j] For fixed i and c, this is a dot product over j of length 49. There are 49 i values and 32 c values, so 49×32 = 1568 outputs per head per window. There are num_heads heads and nWB windows. Total outputs: nWB × num_heads × 49 × 32 For stage 0: 640 × 3 × 1568 = 3,010,560 outputs For stage 3: 10 × 24 × 1568 = 376,320 outputs Each output is a dot product of 49 elements. We can launch one thread per output. - Stage 0: 3M threads = ~3000 blocks of 1024 threads = good - Stage 3: 376k threads = ~368 blocks of 1024 threads = not great but OK Actually, 368 blocks for stage 3 is not bad on H100 with 132 SMs. It's almost 3 blocks per SM. Since each block has 1024 threads and an SM can run 2 blocks (2048 threads max), we get about 1.5 waves. Not terrible. But wait, we also need to handle the window gathering, LayerNorm, and other operations. So maybe we need a more integrated approach. Let me try processing with one thread per output element of the spatial_mlp. But LayerNorm and the shift/unshift need per-position processing. For a cleaner design, I'll split the spatial_mlp into two parts: 1. A kernel that does: shortcut → norm1 → shift → window gather → write to temp buffer 2. A kernel that does: read temp → grouped conv1d → write to output temp → window scatter → unshift → add residual But this is still multiple kernels. Let me try a different strategy: process each window as a tile, with a block per output (window, head, i, c) of the conv. The kernel loads the necessary input data, performs the dot product, and writes the result. Since this is just a matmul, we can implement it as a batched matmul kernel or use existing PyTorch ops. Actually, I just realized: `nn.Conv1d(in_channels, out_channels, kernel_size=1, groups=num_heads)` with input (N, C_in, L) is EQUIVALENT to a grouped matrix multiplication where: - Input is reshaped to (N, num_heads, C_in/num_heads, L) - Weight is reshaped to (num_heads, C_out/num_heads, C_in/num_heads) - Output = einsum('n g i l, g o i -> n g o l', input, weight) This is a batched GEMM over the N×num_heads dimension! In PyTorch, we can do: ```python N, C_in, L = x.shape x_reshaped = x.view(N, num_heads, C_in // num_heads, L) w_reshaped = conv_weight.view(num_heads, C_out // num_heads, C_in // num_heads) # output = einsum('n g i l, g o i -> n g o l', x_reshaped, w_reshaped) output = torch.einsum('ngil,goi->ngol', x_reshaped, w_reshaped) output = output.reshape(N, C_out, L) ``` And PyTorch can optimize `einsum` to use `bmm`. For small matrices, `bmm` might be efficient. But we still have the view/permute/reshape overhead. What if, in the custom kernel, I just implement the spatial_mlp as a direct computation? Let me define the thread organization for `spatial_mlp_fused`: **Grid**: (num_windows, num_heads, ws*ws) **Block**: (C/num_heads) threads, but C/num_heads is 32. Wait, C/num_heads = 32 for all stages. Great constant! So for each (window, head, spatial_position) in the grid: - The block has 32 threads. - Thread c computes output[window, head, spatial_pos, c] - This output = sum_i input[window, head, i, c] * weight[head, spatial_pos, i] Where input is gathered from the padded and window-partitioned input. Wait, the input indexing is complex because of the shift and window partition. For a spatial position (h, w) in window (wh, ww) of batch b: - The corresponding position in the padded input depends on shift_size. - If shift_size > 0: - padded H' = H + P_t + P_b - padded W' = W + P_l + P_r - shifted_h = h + wh * ws + P_t - shifted_w = w + ww * ws + P_l - But for positions near the edges of the window, we need to handle the cyclic shift correctly. Actually, in Swin Transformer, the shift is done by padding and then shifting the window partition. Let me trace the exact indexing. Original code: ```python if self.shift_size > 0: P_l, P_r, P_t, P_b = self.padding shifted_x = F.pad(x, [0, 0, P_l, P_r, P_t, P_b], "constant", 0) else: shifted_x = x ``` Wait, `F.pad(x, [0, 0, P_l, P_r, P_t, P_b])` for x of shape (B, H, W, C): - Pad C dimension by 0 on left, 0 on right (no padding) - Pad W dimension by P_l on left, P_r on right - Pad H dimension by P_t on top, P_b on bottom So padded shape is (B, H+P_t+P_b, W+P_l+P_r, C). Then: ```python x_windows = window_partition(shifted_x, self.window_size) ``` `window_partition`: ```python x = x.view(B, H // ws, ws, W // ws, ws, C) windows = x.permute(0, 1, 3, 2, 4, 5).contiguous().view(-1, ws, ws, C) ``` If `shifted_x` has shape (B, _H, _W, C), then `_H // ws` windows in H, `_W // ws` in W. For shifted windows, positions are indexed normally. The "shift" comes from the padding. For example, with shift_size=3 and ws=7: - P_l = 7-3 = 4, P_r = 3, P_t = 4, P_b = 3 - Original 56×56 is padded to 63×60... wait, H+P_t+P_b = 56+4+3 = 63? But 63 is divisible by 7! W+P_l+P_r = 56+4+3 = 63. So windows are 9×9 = 81 per image. - Without shift, windows are 8×8 = 64 per image. The shift effectively causes cyclic shift by padding and wrapping. After window_partition and conv1d, `window_reverse` reconstructs: ```python x = windows.view(B, H // ws, W // ws, ws, ws, -1) x = x.permute(0, 1, 3, 2, 4, 5).contiguous().view(B, H, W, -1) ``` Then: ```python if self.shift_size > 0: x = shifted_x[:, P_t:-P_b, P_l:-P_r, :].contiguous() ``` So the reverse shift just crops the padding. For the custom kernel, I need to map from output (b, h, w, c) back to the input windows. For a given output position (b, h, w, c): 1. If shift_size == 0: - Window coordinates: wh = h / ws, ww = w / ws - In-window coordinates: ih = h % ws, iw = w % ws - Window index in flattened array: win_idx = b * (H/ws) * (W/ws) + wh * (W/ws) + ww - Input to conv1d for this position: x_windows[win_idx, ih, iw, c] - After conv1d: spatial_mlp[win_idx, ih, iw, c] - Output position: (b, h, w, c) 2. If shift_size > 0: - Padded H' = H + P_t + P_b, W' = W + P_l + P_r - num_win_h = H' / ws, num_win_w = W' / ws - h_padded = h + P_t, w_padded = w + P_l Wait, no. The shift pads the image. Then window partition splits the padded image. But then window reverse reconstructs the padded image, and we crop. - For output (b, h, w), the corresponding position in the padded reconstructed image is (b, h+P_t, w+P_l). - The window for this position: wh = (h+P_t) / ws, ww = (w+P_l) / ws - In-window: ih = (h+P_t) % ws, iw = (w+P_l) % ws - But wait, the padded image is created from the shifted version. Let me think... Actually, the padded image `shifted_x` is created by padding `x` (after norm1). Then `window_partition` splits `shifted_x` into windows. After `spatial_mlp_windows`, `window_reverse` puts them back into `shifted_x` shape. Then we crop `shifted_x` back to `x` shape. So for the output at (b, h, w), the value comes from: - Position (b, h+P_t, w+P_l) in the reconstructed `shifted_x` - Which belongs to window (wh, ww) where wh = (h+P_t) / ws, ww = (w+P_l) / ws - In-window position (ih, iw) where ih = (h+P_t) % ws, iw = (w+P_l) % ws - The conv1d input for this window position was the value at the same in-window position but in the window-partitioned input. For the conv1d input gathering: which window does the padded position (h+P_t, w+P_l) map to? Actually, `shifted_x` is directly window partitioned. Each position in `shifted_x` at (b, hp, wp) maps to window (hp/ws, wp/ws) and offset (hp%ws, wp%ws). The input to the conv1d at window (wh, ww) and offset (ih, iw) is `shifted_x[b, wh*ws+ih, ww*ws+iw, c]`. So for the output position (b, h, w), which maps to padded position (h+P_t, w+P_l): - hp = h+P_t, wp = w+P_l - wh = hp / ws, ww = wp / ws - ih = hp % ws, iw = wp % ws - Input to conv1d = `shifted_x[b, wh*ws+ih, ww*ws+iw, c]` = `shifted_x[b, hp, wp, c]` = `norm1_x[b, (hp-P_t+P_t)%H? No...` Wait, `shifted_x` is just `x` padded with zeros. The padding is `F.pad(x, [0, 0, P_l, P_r, P_t, P_b])`. For position (h+P_t, w+P_l) in `shifted_x`: - If h+P_t < P_t or h+P_t >= H+P_t, i.e., h < 0 or h >= H... but h is in [0, H-1], so h+P_t is in [P_t, H+P_t-1]. Similarly w+P_l is in [P_l, W+P_l-1]. So it's inside the non-padded region. - `shifted_x[b, h+P_t, w+P_l, c] = norm1_x[b, h, w, c]`. - Positions outside [P_t, H+P_t-1] or [P_l, W+P_l-1] would be zeros. Wait, actually `F.pad(x, [0, 0, P_l, P_r, P_t, P_b])`: - PyTorch pad format: starting from the last dimension and moving backward. - `[0, 0]` pads the last dimension (C) by 0 on left and 0 on right. - `[P_l, P_r]` pads the second-to-last dimension (W). - `[P_t, P_b]` pads the third-to-last dimension (H). - So `shifted_x[b, h+P_t, w+P_l, c] = norm1_x[b, h, w, c]` for h in [0, H-1], w in [0, W-1]. - And `shifted_x[b, 0:P_t, :, :]` are zeros, etc. So for output (b, h, w), the conv1d input window containing it is: - hp = h+P_t, wp = w+P_l - wh = hp / ws = (h+P_t) / ws - ww = wp / ws = (w+P_l) / ws - ih = hp % ws = (h+P_t) % ws - iw = wp % ws = (w+P_l) % ws The conv1d operates on all windows. Each window gathers ws×ws spatial positions from `shifted_x`. So for window (wh, ww), the in-window position (ih, iw) comes from `shifted_x[b, wh*ws+ih, ww*ws+iw, c]`. For our output position (b, h, w), we need to find which conv1d output it corresponds to. It's window (wh, ww) and offset (ih, iw) as computed above. The conv1d output for window (wh, ww) at position (ih, iw) and channel c depends on ALL positions in that window, not just (ih, iw). That's why the spatial_mlp is called "spatial" - it mixes information across the window. So for a thread computing output (b, h, w, c): - It needs to know which window (wh, ww) the position belongs to. - It needs to know which head g the channel c belongs to: g = c / (C/num_heads). - It needs to know the within-head channel: cc = c % (C/num_heads). - It needs to load ALL spatial positions in the window for the same head and within-head channel. - Then compute: output_val = sum_{i=0}^{ws*ws-1} window_data[i] * weight[g, spatial_idx_of(h,w), i] + bias[g, spatial_idx_of(h,w)] where spatial_idx_of(h,w) = ih*ws + iw. This means each thread loads ws*ws values from shared memory (the window data) and does a dot product with ws*ws weights. For ws=7, ws*ws=49. Each thread loads 49 floats + 49 weights = ~400 bytes. Very manageable. The challenge is that multiple threads in the same block might need the same window data. We should organize blocks by window. **Block organization for spatial_mlp:** One block per (batch, window_h, window_w, head, channel_in_head)? - That's 10 × 9 × 9 × 3 × 32 = 77760 blocks for stage 0 with shift. Way too many! - Without shift: 10 × 8 × 8 × 3 × 32 = 61440 blocks. Still a lot but better. Actually, we don't need one block per channel. We can have each block process all channels in a head for one window, or all heads for one window. Let me organize by window: - One block per (batch, window_h, window_w, head) - Total blocks without shift: B × (H/ws) × (W/ws) × num_heads - Stage 0: 10 × 8 × 8 × 3 = 1920 blocks - Stage 1: 10 × 4 × 4 × 6 = 960 blocks - Stage 2: 10 × 2 × 2 × 12 = 480 blocks - Stage 3: 10 × 1 × 1 × 24 = 240 blocks Each block processes all spatial positions (ws*ws = 49) and all channels per head (C/num_heads = 32). For stages with different ws: - ws=7, ws*ws=49. 49×16=784 threads works for C/nH=32. - But later stages also have ws=7 (window_size is constant across stages). So we can use a grid of (B, num_win_h, num_win_w, num_heads) and blocks of (7, 7, 16) = 784 threads. For stage 3 with 240 blocks and 784 threads each: 188k threads. On 132 SMs with 2048 threads each, that's less than 1 wave. Utilization: 188k / 270k = 70%. Not bad. For stage 3 with shift_size=3: - num_win_h = (7+4+3)/7 = 14/7 = 2? Wait, H=7, P_t=4, P_b=3. _H = 7+4+3 = 14. num_win_h = 2. Similarly _W = 14, num_win_w = 2. - Blocks: 10 × 2 × 2 × 24 = 960 blocks. Much better! 960 × 784 = 752k threads. Excellent utilization. For stage 2 with H=W=14, shift_size=0 or 3: - Without shift: num_win = 2×2 = 4. Blocks: 10×4×12 = 480. - With shift: _H = 14+4+3 = 21. num_win = 3×3 = 9. Blocks: 10×9×12 = 1080. So the number of blocks is always reasonable when shift > 0, and acceptable when shift = 0 (though stage 3 without shift has only 240 blocks). Inside each block, the threads will: 1. Load the window data from the padded input into shared memory 2. Load the conv weights for this head into shared memory 3. Each thread computes its assigned outputs 4. Write results to the output buffer Wait, we also need to handle the LayerNorm1 and the residual add! For the LayerNorm1: each spatial position needs to be normalized across all C channels. But our block only processes one head (C/num_heads = 32 channels out of 96/192/384/768 total). So we can't compute LayerNorm in this block unless we have all channels. Unless we first launch a separate LayerNorm kernel, or organize blocks differently. If we organize blocks by window (not by head), with all heads in one block: - Stage 0: C=96, ws*ws=49. Total elements per window = 49×96 = 4704. - With 1024 threads, each thread handles ~4.6 elements. Not a natural multiple. - We could use 96 threads (one per channel) and each thread loops over 49 spatial positions. But then the matmul becomes awkward. What if we first compute LayerNorm in a separate kernel, then call the window kernel? - LayerNorm kernel: one block per spatial position (B×H×W blocks). - Stage 0: 31360 blocks of 96 threads = good - Stage 3: 490 blocks of 768 threads = OK Actually, LayerNorm can be done with a single kernel call and PyTorch has an optimized implementation. But custom fusion might help. Hmm, this is getting quite complex to manage in a single kernel. Let me think about whether I can write a simpler but still effective kernel. ### Revised Simpler Strategy Given the complexity of perfectly fusing everything, I'll implement the following practical approach: 1. **Extract all weights** from the `Model` into the module for easy access 2. **Override `forward`** to use custom kernels 3. **Custom kernels**: - `spatial_mlp_fused`: Replaces the window_partition → conv1d → window_reverse path. Operates on already-normalized input. Handles shift/unshift and the grouped matmul internally. - `mlp_fused`: Replaces norm2 → MLP → residual. Calls PyTorch for FC1, but fuses GELU → FC2 → Add in one kernel to save one memory round-trip. For (3), I can also add a `patch_merging_fused` kernel. Actually, let me focus more broadly. What if I replace each `SwinMLPBlock` with a Python function that: 1. Does the spatial path using a custom kernel 2. Does the MLP path using another custom kernel (with internal matmuls) And for the MLP custom kernel, I'll implement the matmuls as simple loops. I'll tune the thread block size to handle different C efficiently. Let me write this kernel carefully: **MLP Kernel:** ```cpp __global__ void mlp_fused_kernel( const float* input, float* output, const float* norm_w, const float* norm_b, const float* fc1_w, const float* fc1_b, const float* fc2_w, const float* fc2_b, int N, int C, int H ) { // N = B * L (number of rows) // C = input/output channels // H = hidden dim int row = blockIdx.x; if (row >= N) return; extern __shared__ float smem[]; float* smem_input = smem; // C elements float* smem_inter = smem + C; // H elements float* smem_mean_var = smem_inter + H; // 2 elements for mean and inv_std // Load input const float* input_row = input + row * C; for (int i = threadIdx.x; i < C; i += blockDim.x) { smem_input[i] = input_row[i]; } __syncthreads(); // LayerNorm float local_sum = 0; for (int i = threadIdx.x; i < C; i += blockDim.x) { local_sum += smem_input[i]; } // Block reduce sum __shared__ float s_mean, s_invstd; // ... reduce to get mean // ... reduce to get var // ... normalize __syncthreads(); // FC1: smem_input (C) -> smem_inter (H) // Each thread computes some output elements for (int out_idx = threadIdx.x; out_idx < H; out_idx += blockDim.x) { float acc = fc1_b[out_idx]; // Dot product with input for (int c = 0; c < C; c++) { acc += smem_input[c] * fc1_w[out_idx * C + c]; } smem_inter[out_idx] = acc; } __syncthreads(); // GELU for (int i = threadIdx.x; i < H; i += blockDim.x) { float x = smem_inter[i]; // GELU implementation float cdf = 0.5f * (1.0f + tanhf(0.7978845608f * (x + 0.044715f * x * x * x))); smem_inter[i] = x * cdf; } __syncthreads(); // FC2: smem_inter (H) -> smem_input (C) for (int out_idx = threadIdx.x; out_idx < C; out_idx += blockDim.x) { float acc = fc2_b[out_idx]; for (int h = 0; h < H; h++) { acc += smem_inter[h] * fc2_w[out_idx * H + h]; } // Add residual (input before norm) acc += input_row[out_idx]; output[row * C + out_idx] = acc; } } ``` Wait, I need to be careful with the weight layout. `fc1_w` from PyTorch `nn.Linear(C, H)` has shape (H, C) and is stored as `fc1_w[h * C + c]`. `fc2_w` from `nn.Linear(H, C)` has shape (C, H) and is stored as `fc2_w[c * H + h]`. For `fc1_w`, the access pattern in the kernel should be `fc1_w[out_idx * C + c]`, which matches our loop. For `fc2_w`, the access pattern is `fc2_w[out_idx * H + h]`, which is also good for a fixed out_idx and varying h. The issue with this kernel is register pressure and warp divergence. For the FC1 loop: ```cpp for (int out_idx = threadIdx.x; out_idx < H; out_idx += blockDim.x) { float acc = fc1_b[out_idx]; for (int c = 0; c < C; c++) { acc += smem_input[c] * fc1_w[out_idx * C + c]; } smem_inter[out_idx] = acc; } ``` If blockDim.x = 256 and H = 3072, each thread iterates 12 times for out_idx. Inside, the inner loop over C=768 runs 768 times. Total work per thread: 12 × 768 = 9216 FMAs. Plus loading weights: 12 × 768 floats = 9216 floats = 36 KB per thread. This is way too much for registers. The weights can't all be loaded into registers at once. But they're loaded one at a time in the inner loop: `fc1_w[out_idx * C + c]`. For a fixed out_idx, as c varies, the loads are consecutive. The compiler keeps just a few values in registers. As long as the compiler doesn't unroll the loop aggressively, register pressure should be fine. Similarly for FC2: each thread computes C/blockDim.x outputs. With C=768 and blockDim=256, each computes 3 outputs. For each output, it loops over H=3072, loading `fc2_w[out_idx * H + h]` consecutively. Total loads: 3 × 3072 = 9216 floats. Register pressure should be OK if the compiler doesn't unroll. Now, about the `tanhf` in GELU: `tanhf` is a built-in CUDA intrinsic but it's expensive. We can use a fast approximation or the exact formula. For numerical equivalence with PyTorch's GELU, we need to check what formula PyTorch uses. PyTorch's GELU: ```python 0.5 * x * (1 + torch.tanh(math.sqrt(2 / math.pi) * (x + 0.044715 * torch.pow(x, 3)))) ``` This is the `tanh` approximation, not the exact `erf` version. Some implementations use different approximations. I should implement the exact PyTorch formula to ensure correctness. Actually, I just checked: PyTorch's `nn.GELU` without the `approximate` parameter uses the exact formula based on `erf` by default. Wait, no - let me check. In PyTorch, `nn.GELU()` uses the exact GELU by default: ```python x * 0.5 * (1.0 + torch.erf(x / math.sqrt(2))) ``` But there's also `torch.nn.functional.gelu(x, approximate='none'/'tanh')`. The module `nn.GELU` defaults to `approximate='none'`, which uses `erf`. Wait, actually in newer PyTorch versions, `nn.GELU` defaults to `approximate='none'`. Let me verify by checking what the reference uses. The reference says: ```python class Mlp(nn.Module): def __init__(self, in_features, hidden_features=None, out_features=None, act_layer=nn.GELU, drop=0.): ... self.act = act_layer() ``` So it uses `nn.GELU()`. In PyTorch, `nn.GELU` without arguments uses the exact formula by default (at least in recent versions). But the exact formula with `erf` is expensive. Let me check the `nn.GELU` docs... Actually, `nn.GELU` has an `approximate` parameter. In PyTorch 2.x, the default is `approximate='none'`, which means exact. But in some versions it might be `tanh`. Hmm, to be safe and fast, I'll implement the tanh approximation since it's what many fast implementations use, and check if it matches the reference. The tolerance is atol=rtol=1e-4 for fp32, so approximation should be fine. Actually, let me check what PyTorch actually does. In PyTorch source, `nn.GELU` maps to `at::gelu`. The implementation uses: - For `approximate='none'`: `x * 0.5 * (1 + erf(x / sqrt(2)))` - For `approximate='tanh'`: `x * 0.5 * (1 + tanh(sqrt(2/π) * (x + 0.044715 * x^3)))` The module default is `approximate='none'` in PyTorch 2.0+. For numerical equivalence within 1e-4, the tanh approximation might or might not be acceptable. The difference between exact and tanh GELU can be up to ~1e-5 for typical inputs, which is within 1e-4. So the approximation should be fine. But to be safe, I should use the exact same implementation as PyTorch. Since the reference uses `nn.GELU()` which defaults to exact, I should implement the exact version. However, `erf` in CUDA is `erff()` and it's relatively slow. For a memory-bound kernel, this might matter. Actually, `erff()` is a hardware-supported instruction on H100? No, it's typically a software function, though it might use some specialized instructions. Let me use the exact formula to start with, and if performance is bad, switch to approximation. ```cpp // Exact GELU float gelu_exact(float x) { return 0.5f * x * (1.0f + erff(x * 0.7071067811865476f)); } ``` Wait, `erff` might not match PyTorch's exactly in terms of numerical precision. But it should be very close. Actually, let me check: does PyTorch use `erf` or `erfc` or some approximation? It uses `at::erf`. The CUDA implementation probably calls `erff` from libdevice. I'll implement exact GELU with `erff` for correctness. Now, for the actual kernel launch config, how do I choose `blockDim.x`? For C=96: - LayerNorm: reduce 96 elements. Use 96 or 128 threads. - FC1: 96 -> 384. Each thread computes 384/128 = 3 outputs. - FC2: 384 -> 96. Each thread computes 96/128 = 0.75 outputs. Not integer. Use 96 threads instead. Actually, I'll use a dynamic block size. For each stage, I'll choose `blockDim.x = next_power_of_2(max(C, H))` capped at 1024. Wait, but a block needs to handle BOTH C and H dimensions. The thread count should divide both reasonably, or at least handle one efficiently. If I use `blockDim.x = 128`: - For C=96, layer norm: 128 threads, but only 96 elements. Some threads idle. Fine. - FC1 from 96 to 384: 384/128 = 3 outputs per thread. Good. - FC2 from 384 to 96: 96/128 = 0.75. Thread divergence. What if blockDim.x = 256? Same issue for C=96. What if I make the block size equal to `max(C, H)` or the next power of 2? - C=96, H=384: block=384. - LayerNorm: 384 threads, 96 elements. Each thread loads at most 1 element. 3/4 threads do nothing in the norm loop. Inefficient. Better: blockDim = 128 for most cases. Handle non-divisible by having threads process multiple outputs in strides. Like: ```cpp for (int out_idx = threadIdx.x; out_idx < H; out_idx += blockDim.x) { // compute output out_idx } ``` This always works without divergence, but the loop may run multiple times per thread. For blockDim=128, H=384: each thread computes 3 outputs (128, 256, 384 if threadIdx < something). Wait, threadIdx=0 computes out_idx 0, 128, 256. ThreadIdx=1 computes 1, 129, 257. ... ThreadIdx=127 computes 127, 255, 383. Each computes exactly 3 outputs. No divergence. For C=96, H=384, blockDim=128: - FC1: 128 threads, each 3 outputs. Total 384. Good. - FC2: C=96, 128 threads. Each thread computes 0 or 1 outputs? With the strided loop: ```cpp for (int out_idx = threadIdx.x; out_idx < C; out_idx += blockDim.x) { ``` Thread 0: 0. Thread 1: 1. ... Thread 95: 95. Threads 96-127: no work. So 32/128 threads are idle. That's 25% idle. Acceptable. For C=768, H=3072, blockDim=1024: - FC1: each thread computes 3 outputs (0, 1024, 2048). Good. - FC2: each thread computes 1 output. Perfect. For C=768, H=3072, blockDim=768: - FC1: each thread computes 4 outputs (0, 768, 1536, 2304). Good. - FC2: each thread computes 1 output. Good. So `blockDim.x = min(1024, max(C, H))` might work, but let me think about what minimizes idle time. For (C, H) pairs: - (96, 384): max=384. block=256 or 128. With 256, FC1: each thread 1.5 outputs - not integer with stride. Actually with stride: - thread 0: 0, 256 - thread 1: 1, 257 - ... - thread 127: 127, 383 - thread 128: 128 (only 1 output since 384 < 512) - So threads 128-255 compute 1 output, threads 0-127 compute 2 outputs. Divergence! To avoid this, blockDim should divide H (for FC1) and C (for FC2) as evenly as possible. For (96, 384): H=384 is divisible by 128. C=96 is not divisible by 384. But blockDim only needs to be ≤ C for FC2 to avoid divergence. If blockDim > C, some threads are idle. - blockDim=128: 96 threads active in FC2, 32 idle. 75% util. - blockDim=96: 384/96 = 4 outputs per thread in FC1. Perfect for both. And H/C always equals mlp_ratio=4, so each thread computes exactly 4 outputs in FC1. This is perfect! Let me use blockDim = C. Wait, but there's one issue: then smem for fc1 output needs H elements. If C=96, H=384. Shared memory: input 96 + inter 384 + a bit more = ~2KB. Very small. For C=768, H=3072: shared = 768 + 3072 + 2 = ~16KB. Also small. So blockDim=C is great. But what about stages where shift_size > 0 and we need the spatial_mlp kernel? For that kernel, we might want blockDim = C/num_heads = 32. OK, let me now write the full kernel strategy. ### Final Kernel Design **Kernel 1: `spatial_mlp_fused`** Fuses: norm1 (but wait, we said blockDim needs to be C for norm. For spatial_mlp, we need window-level cooperation). Actually, if I want ONE kernel per SwinMLPBlock, I need to handle both norm1 (needs all C channels per position) and spatial_mlp (needs all positions per window). I could do: 1. Compute norm1 in a separate kernel, or 2. Have each block handle one spatial position for norm1, then synchronize somehow to gather window data, or 3. Launch blocks for each window, with each block having C threads. For one window: - C threads (one per channel) - LayerNorm: each thread loads one value, then block reduces mean and var across C threads. - Then all threads have the normalized window in shared memory. - For the shift: each thread knows its (h, w) and can compute the corresponding padded position. - Wait, but the padded position for a window depends on the window index. Actually, I realize it's hard to have C threads compute LayerNorm and spatial_mlp together efficiently because: - LayerNorm needs all C channels per position. With C threads in a block, each thread handles one channel across ALL positions in the window. - But spatial_mlp's grouped conv needs all channels per head across all positions. The matmul is per-head, so threads in the same head need to cooperate. What if a block handles one window, with C threads? - Load the window: ws*ws positions × C channels. Total ws*ws*C floats. - Stage 0: 49*96 = 4704 floats = 18.75 KB. Fits in shared memory. - Stage 3: 49*768 = 37632 floats = 150 KB. Also fits! (max 227 KB) - Each thread handles one channel across all positions. - LayerNorm: for each position, all C threads participate. We need to loop over positions. - For pos=0 to ws*ws-1: - Load all C channels for this position into a temporary shared array - Compute mean and var across C threads - Normalize and store back - This requires C ≤ 1024, which is true for all stages (max C=768). - Then apply the grouped conv/spatial_mlp. - For each head g, for each position i, for each channel c in head: - output[g, i, c] = sum_j window[g, j, c] * weight[g, i, j] + bias[g, i] - With C threads, each thread handles one channel. But each channel belongs to one head. - Thread c processes all positions i for its channel c. - For a fixed c, the thread loops over i=0..ws*ws-1 and computes dot product over j=0..ws*ws-1. - This requires loading weight[g, i, j] for each i and j. - Weight size per head: ws*ws × ws*ws = 2401 floats = ~10 KB. - For C=768 and num_heads=24, there are 24 distinct weight matrices. If a block has 768 threads, each head has 32 threads. We could store all 24 weight matrices in shared memory: 24 * 2401 * 4 = 230 KB. That exceeds shared memory for stage 3! So storing all weights in shared memory doesn't work for stage 3. But each thread only needs the weight matrix for its head. We can stream weight rows or have each thread load weights from global memory. Since weights are read-only and global memory is cached in L2, each thread can load its weights on demand. For thread c: - head = c / (C/num_heads) - Load weight_head[0:ws*ws*ws*ws] when needed. - For each output position i: - acc = bias[head, i] - For j=0 to ws*ws-1: - acc += window[head, j, c_in_head] * weight[head, i, j] - output[head, i, c_in_head] = acc For ws=7, ws*ws=49. Each dot product is 49 multiplies and adds. Very fast. Weight access: 49 floats per i, 49 i values, total 2401 floats = ~10 KB per thread. But wait, consecutive threads in the same warp might belong to different heads. If they do, they access different weight matrices, potentially thrashing the cache. However, threads in the same head are consecutive: if C/nH=32, then threads 0-31 are head 0, threads 32-63 are head 1, etc. So within a warp of 32 threads, all threads are in the SAME head (since 32 = C/nH). This is perfect! For a warp of 32 threads all in the same head: - They all access the SAME weight matrix. Excellent cache behavior. - They access the SAME window data for the j positions, but different channels c. - For a fixed i, thread c loads weight[head, i, j] which is the same for all threads in the warp. This can benefit from warp-level broadcasting or L1 cache. - Thread c loads window[head, j, c] which is different. With 32 threads loading 32 consecutive channels, this is fully coalesced. This is a great memory access pattern! For the dot product over j=0..48: - At each j, the warp collectively loads window[head, j, 0:31] which is 32 consecutive floats = 128 bytes = one cache line. Fully coalesced. - One thread (or all threads) loads weight[head, i, j] which is broadcast to all. - Each thread does one FMA. - 49 FMAs per output position i. - With 32 channels and 49 output positions, each thread does 49 FMAs. Wait, each thread computes one output element for each of the 49 positions? So 49 FMAs per thread? That's very little compute. Actually, each thread handles one channel. For each of the 49 output positions i, it computes a dot product of length 49. So each thread does 49*49 = 2401 FMAs. Plus 49 bias adds. Total ~2400 FMAs per thread. With 768 threads in a block: 768 * 2401 = 1.84M FMAs per block. For stage 3 with 10 blocks: 18.4M FMAs. At 67 TFLOPS = 0.27 microseconds. The memory loads will dominate. For stage 0 with 640 blocks: 640 * 1.84M = 1.18B FMAs. At 67 TFLOPS = 17.6 microseconds. Better. But the issue is the number of blocks. For stage 3, 10 blocks. Each with 768 threads = 7680 threads total. H100 has ~132 SMs × 2048 threads = 270k threads capacity. So only 2.8% utilization. This would be VERY slow. Wait, I miscalculated. Stage 3 num_windows: - H=7, W=7, ws=7. Without shift: 1 window per image. With 10 images: 10 windows. - With shift: padded to H+P_t+P_b = 7+4+3 = 14. _H/ws = 2. Similarly _W/ws = 2. Total windows = 2*2 = 4 per image. With 10 images: 40 windows. So depending on shift_size, stage 3 has 10 or 40 windows. Still low. Actually, wait. The blocks I described above process one window with C threads. So the number of blocks equals the number of windows. This is: - Stage 0: 10 × 8 × 8 = 640 (or 10 × 9 × 9 = 810 with shift) - Stage 1: 10 × 4 × 4 = 160 (or 10 × 5 × 5 = 250) - Stage 2: 10 × 2 × 2 = 40 (or 10 × 3 × 3 = 90) - Stage 3: 10 × 1 × 1 = 10 (or 10 × 2 × 2 = 40) These are the number of windows. Good. - Stage 2: 40 × 384 = 15,360 threads. Low. - Stage 3: 10 × 768 = 7,680 threads. Very low. To improve utilization for later stages, we could have multiple blocks per window. For stage 3 with 10 windows, if each block processes 2 windows, we have 5 blocks. Still low. If each block processes all 10 windows: 1 block with 768 threads. Terrible. What if we first compute LayerNorm in a separate kernel (one block per spatial position), then launch the spatial_mlp as a separate kernel? **Two-kernel approach for spatial_mlp:** 1. `layernorm_kernel`: one block per spatial position (B×H×W blocks), C threads each.
