Actually, if the memory layout is the main cost, I can write a custom kernel where each thread computes one output ELEMENT (after the spatial_mlp). This gives very high parallelism. **Element-wise spatial_mlp kernel:** Grid: (num_elements) where num_elements = B × H × W × C. Block: 256 or 512 threads. For each output element (b, h, w, c): 1. Determine which window it belongs to (with shift). 2. Determine the in-window position (ih, iw). 3. Determine the head g and within-head channel cc. 4. Load all ws*ws values in the window for (b, window, g, cc). 5. Compute dot product with weight[g, ih*ws+iw, :]. 6. Add bias[g, ih*ws+iw]. 7. Write to output. But step 4 requires loading ws*ws values. If many threads in the same warp try to load the same window data, there's memory contention. But if they're accessing different windows, it's fine. With B×H×W×C elements: - Stage 0: 10 × 56 × 56 × 96 = 30,105,600 elements. Great parallelism. - Stage 3: 10 × 7 × 7 × 768 = 376,320 elements. Moderate parallelism. This gives much better parallelism! But each thread loads ws*ws values. For ws=7, that's 49 loads per thread. If threads in the same warp access the same window, they share some loads. But if they access different windows, each loads 49 values independently. For stage 3 with 376k elements and 256 threads per block: ~1470 blocks. Each block has 256 threads. Each thread 49 loads. Total = 72M loads. With L2 cache, if the same windows are accessed by many threads, cache helps. Wait, each window has 49 × 768 = 37,632 elements. But each output element only reads 49 elements (one per spatial position in the window, for the same channel). So each window is read 49 × 768 = 37,632 times (once per output element in the window). But all these reads are for the SAME 49 × 768 elements. If threads in the same warp access different output positions that happen to be in the same window, the input window data can be cached in L1. But threads in a warp are consecutive, so if we're processing elements in order, consecutive elements are in the same window until we reach the window boundary. For row-major layout of (B, H, W, C): - Consecutive elements in C are in the same spatial position. - After C elements, we move to the next spatial position. If we process elements in order: - Threads 0-255 process elements (b, h, w, 0:255). But C might be 768. So some threads process different channels in the same position. - But for the spatial_mlp, all channels in the same position need to read the same window data! - Threads in the same warp (0-31) might process channels 0-31, which are all in head 0 and within-head channels 0-31. They all need to read the same window data (all 49 positions for head 0, channels 0-31). Wait, but each thread reads 49 values. Thread 0 reads window[:, 0], thread 1 reads window[:, 1], etc. These are in the same window and same channels? No! Actually, for output position (h, w, c), the required input is: `input[window_j, c]` for all j=0..48. So thread processing channel c reads the values of channel c at all 49 window positions. If threads 0-31 process channels 0-31 of the same spatial position: - Thread 0 reads window[:, 0] - Thread 1 reads window[:, 1] - ... - Thread 31 reads window[:, 31] - These are 32 different channels, strided by C across the window data. In memory, window data is stored as (num_windows, ws, ws, C) or similar. If consecutive channels are stored together, then thread 0 accessing channel 0 and thread 1 accessing channel 1 are accessing adjacent memory. But they're accessing different spatial positions within the window. Actually, window data for a single window is (ws*ws, C) in row-major if we reshape. Or (ws, ws, C). For position j in the window and channel c: offset = j * C + c. Thread c accesses offset j*C + c for j=0..48. With C=768, offsets are 0, 768, 1536, ... for thread 0. Thread 1: 1, 769, 1537, ... Thread 31: 31, 799, 1535+31, ... Consecutive threads access offsets that differ by 1. But these are at different j positions, so they're not adjacent in memory (stride is C = 768 floats = 3 KB). A warp accessing 32 elements spaced 3KB apart touches 32 different cache lines. This is terrible for memory bandwidth! To fix this, we need the threads to access adjacent memory. That means consecutive threads should process the same channel but different positions, or consecutive positions in the same channel. But for the dot product, each thread needs 49 values for ONE channel to compute ONE output. If consecutive threads process different positions (i) of the same channel, they need 49 different 49-load dot products. Wait, the dot product for output (h, w, c) uses weight row i and input window column c. There's no easy way to make the memory access pattern good without rethinking the entire algorithm. For a single (window, head), the operation is essentially a matmul: output = input @ weight.T where input is (ws*ws, C/nH) and weight is (ws*ws, ws*ws). This is a standard matmul! We should implement it as a matmul, not element-by-element. For the whole batch: (nWB, num_heads, ws*ws, C/nH) @ (num_heads, ws*ws, ws*ws) This is a batched matmul over nWB, with each batch being (num_heads, ws*ws, C/nH) @ (num_heads, ws*ws, ws*ws). Actually, since num_heads is also a batch dimension, it's a batched matmul with batch = nWB * num_heads. PyTorch's `bmm` can handle this. In fact, we can reshape the input and weight and call `bmm`. ```python # Instead of all the views and conv1d: # x_windows is (nWB, ws, ws, C) # x_windows_heads = x_windows.view(nWB, ws*ws, num_heads, C//num_heads).permute(0, 2, 1, 3) # -> (nWB, num_heads, ws*ws, C//num_heads) # conv_weight is (num_heads*ws*ws, ws*ws, 1) in Conv1d # But for matmul, we need weight as (num_heads, ws*ws, ws*ws) # Actually Conv1d weight is (C_out, C_in/groups, K) = (num_heads*ws*ws, ws*ws, 1) # Reshape: (num_heads, ws*ws, ws*ws, 1) -> squeeze -> (num_heads, ws*ws, ws*ws) # Then: # output = torch.matmul(x_windows_heads, conv_weight_reshaped.transpose(-2, -1)) # output = output.permute(0, 2, 1, 3).reshape(nWB, ws*ws, C) ``` The matmul dimensions: - x_windows_heads: (nWB, num_heads, ws*ws, C/nH) - conv_weight_reshaped: (num_heads, ws*ws, ws*ws) - The matmul should be: (nWB, num_heads, ws*ws, C/nH) × (num_heads, ws*ws, ws*ws) In einsum: `n h i c, h o i -> n h o c` where n=nWB, h=num_heads, i=ws*ws, c=C/nH, o=ws*ws. This is exactly a batched matrix multiply with batch dimensions (n, h). The matrix dimensions are (i, c) × (o, i).T = (i, c) × (i, o). No wait. `x_windows_heads` is (n, h, i, c). `conv_weight` is (h, o, i) where o = ws*ws. The conv1d computes: output[n, h, o, c] = sum_i input[n, h, i, c] * weight[h, o, i] This is einsum `n h i c, h o i -> n h o c`. In matrix terms, for fixed n and h: input_matrix is (i, c), weight_matrix is (o, i). output_matrix = weight_matrix @ input_matrix. weight is (o, i), input is (i, c). Output is (o, c). So `output[n,h] = weight[h] @ input[n,h]`. The batch dimensions are n and h. So total batches = nWB × num_heads. Each batch matmul is (ws*ws, ws*ws) @ (ws*ws, C/nH) = (49, 49) @ (49, 32). This is a tiny matmul! cuBLAS batched GEMM handles this. Using `einsum` or `bmm` in PyTorch would be much more efficient than the Conv1d + all the reshapes. Let me rewrite the spatial_mlp path using `bmm`: ```python # Original: x_windows_heads = x_windows.view(-1, ws*ws, num_heads, C//num_heads).transpose(1, 2) x_windows_heads = x_windows_heads.reshape(-1, num_heads * ws*ws, C // num_heads) spatial_mlp_windows = self.spatial_mlp(x_windows_heads) spatial_mlp_windows = spatial_mlp_windows.view(-1, num_heads, ws*ws, C//num_heads).transpose(1, 2) spatial_mlp_windows = spatial_mlp_windows.reshape(-1, ws*ws, C) # Optimized: x_perm = x_windows.view(-1, ws*ws, num_heads, C//num_heads).permute(0, 2, 1, 3) # x_perm: (nWB, num_heads, ws*ws, C//num_heads) conv_w = self.spatial_mlp.weight.view(num_heads, ws*ws, ws*ws) # conv_w: (num_heads, ws*ws, ws*ws) conv_b = self.spatial_mlp.bias.view(num_heads, ws*ws) # Use bmm output = torch.matmul(conv_w, x_perm.transpose(-2, -1)) # (nWB, num_heads, ws*ws, ws*ws) @ (nWB, num_heads, C//num_heads, ws*ws).T # Wait, dimensions don't match for bmm directly. Let me think. ``` For `bmm` to work, we need: batch1: (b, o, i) batch2: (b, i, c) output: (b, o, c) Here, for each (n, h), we have: - weight: (o, i) = (ws*ws, ws*ws) - input: (i, c) = (ws*ws, C/nH) - output: (o, c) = (ws*ws, C/nH) So if we reshape input to (nWB * num_heads, ws*ws, C/nH) and weight to (nWB * num_heads, ws*ws, ws*ws) [repeating weight for each n], then `bmm(weight_batched, input)` gives (nWB*num_heads, ws*ws, C/nH). Or we can use `einsum` which is optimized to `bmm` internally: ```python output = torch.einsum('hoi,nhic->nhoc', conv_w, x_perm) ``` PyTorch's `einsum` will dispatch to `bmm` if possible. This avoids all the manual reshapes. Then: - output shape: (nWB, num_heads, ws*ws, C/nH) - Permute back: `output.permute(0, 2, 1, 3).reshape(nWB, ws*ws, C)` This replaces the entire conv1d + all views with ONE einsum call. `einsum` handles the batching efficiently. For a tiny matmul of (49, 49) @ (49, 32), batched across 1920 instances (stage 0), `bmm` should be very efficient. And we avoid the `view/permute/contiguous/view/reshape/transpose` mess entirely. The remaining overhead is the `window_partition` and `window_reverse` which do `view/permute/contiguous`. These create copies! If we can write custom kernels for window_partition and window_reverse that write directly in the required layout, we can save significant time. Actually, if we use `einsum` with the right input layout, maybe we can avoid some permutes. Original `window_partition`: ```python x.view(B, H//ws, ws, W//ws, ws, C).permute(0, 1, 3, 2, 4, 5).contiguous().view(-1, ws, ws, C) ``` This gathers data into windows. The permute changes memory layout. contiguous() copies. Custom `window_partition_kernel` can directly write windows in the layout needed. But `einsum` expects a specific layout. Actually, if `einsum` uses `bmm` internally, it might transpose automatically. Actually, what if we call `einsum` directly on the padded input without explicit window_partition? For position (nWH, ih, iw, head, channel), we need to map to the padded image: - n = nWH / (num_win_h * num_win_w) - wh = (nWH % (num_win_h * num_win_w)) / num_win_w - ww = (nWH % (num_win_h * num_win_w)) % num_win_w - h = wh * ws + ih - w = ww * ws + iw The input to einsum needs to be (nWB, num_heads, ws*ws, C/nH) for the bmm approach. A custom kernel can load the window data and write it directly in this layout. Then `einsum` or a custom bmm can operate on it. But for `einsum`, we still need a separate kernel call. So the sequence would be: 1. custom `window_gather` kernel: writes (nWB, num_heads, ws*ws, C/nH) 2. `einsum` or `matmul`: computes (nWB, num_heads, ws*ws, C/nH) → (nWB, num_heads, ws*ws, C/nH) 3. custom `window_scatter` kernel: writes back to (B, H, W, C) Steps 1 and 3 replace `window_partition+view/permute` and `view/permute+window_reverse`. Step 2 replaces `conv1d+more_views`. If we can fuse step 2 into a custom kernel too, that would be even better. But the batched tiny matmul might actually be handled well by cuBLAS. Wait, what if I write one kernel that does BOTH window_gather and the matmul? Each thread block handles one (nWB, head) pair. The block loads 49 × 32 values, computes the 49 × 32 output using the 49 × 49 weight, and writes the result. Then another kernel does window_scatter and unshift + residual. This completely eliminates the intermediate tensor between window_gather and matmul. Let me write this kernel: ```cpp __global__ void window_mlp_kernel( const float* padded_input, float* window_output, const float* weight, const float* bias, int B, int _H, int _W, int C, int num_heads, int ws ) { // Grid: (nWB, num_heads) // Block: (ws*ws, C/num_heads) but C/nH=32, ws*ws=49, so 49*32=1568 > 1024. // Instead: blockDim = (C/num_heads, 1) = 32 threads? Then each thread computes ws*ws outputs. // Or blockDim = (ws*ws, 1) = 49 threads, each computes C/nH=32 outputs. int nwb = blockIdx.x; // which window across all batches int head = blockIdx.y; int nH = num_heads; int ch_per_head = C / nH; // Determine window position in padded image int windows_per_img = (_H / ws) * (_W / ws); int b = nwb / windows_per_img; int win_id = nwb % windows_per_img; int wh = win_id / (_W / ws); int ww = win_id % (_W / ws); // Load window data into shared memory // shape: (ws*ws, ch_per_head) __shared__ float smem_window[49 * 32]; // max size for ws=7, ch=32 // Each thread loads some elements for (int idx = threadIdx.x; idx < ws*ws*ch_per_head; idx += blockDim.x) { int pos = idx / ch_per_head; int ch = idx % ch_per_head; int ih = pos / ws; int iw = pos % ws; int h = wh * ws + ih; int w = ww * ws + iw; int input_idx = ((b * _H + h) * _W + w) * C + head * ch_per_head + ch; smem_window[pos * ch_per_head + ch] = padded_input[input_idx]; } __syncthreads(); // Now compute output: for each pos and each ch, dot product with weight // weight shape for this head: (ws*ws, ws*ws) // Each thread computes one or more (pos, ch) outputs for (int idx = threadIdx.x; idx < ws*ws*ch_per_head; idx += blockDim.x) { int pos = idx / ch_per_head; int ch = idx % ch_per_head; float acc = bias[head * ws*ws + pos]; for (int j = 0; j < ws*ws; j++) { acc += smem_window[j * ch_per_head + ch] * weight[(head * ws*ws + pos) * ws*ws + j]; } window_output[((nwb * nH + head) * ws*ws + pos) * ch_per_head + ch] = acc; } } ``` This kernel: - Grid: (nWB, num_heads) - Block: 256 or 512 threads - Each block processes one (window, head) pair - Loads the window into shared memory - Computes all outputs for that window and head - Writes to a temporary buffer Then a second kernel scatters this back to the image. For stage 0: nWB=640, nH=3. Grid: (640, 3). Total blocks = 1920. Each with 256 threads. For stage 3: nWB=10, nH=24. Grid: (10, 24). Total blocks = 240. 240 blocks is not great, but the compute per block is decent: 49*32 = 1568 outputs, each 49 FMAs. Total per block: 1568*49 = ~77k FMAs. At 67 TFLOPS, 240 blocks × 77k = 18.5M FMAs = 0.27 microseconds. But with 240 blocks, some SMs are idle. Could take ~10-50 microseconds due to underutilization. Actually, in practice, batched small GEMMs via cuBLAS might handle 240 batches efficiently. But our custom kernel might not. For 240 blocks of 256 threads = 61,440 threads. On H100, this is about 22% thread capacity. Not great, but not terrible either. Hmm, 240 blocks and 61k threads is very low for H100. But if each block does enough work (77k FMAs), maybe the GPU can execute them quickly. A single H100 SM can do ~500 GFLOPS (FP32). At that rate, 77k FMAs = 154k FLOPs per block = 0.3 microseconds per block per SM. With 132 SMs handling 240 blocks concurrently (some SMs get 2 blocks), total time ≈ 2 waves × 0.3 microseconds = 0.6 microseconds. The memory loads dominate. In practice, with memory overhead, maybe 5-10 microseconds. Still very fast. And the savings come from avoiding the view/permute/contiguous overhead of PyTorch. Then the second kernel (window_scatter) also launches 1920 or 240 blocks and is similarly fast. For the MLP, I'll write the fused kernel with blockDim=C as discussed. OK, I think I have a viable strategy. Let me now draft the full Python module. ### Implementation Plan ```python import torch import torch.nn as nn from torch.utils.cpp_extension import load_inline # CUDA source cuda_source = ''' ... CUDA kernels ... ''' # Define PyTorch-binding functions cpp_source = ''' torch::Tensor spatial_mlp_forward(...); torch::Tensor mlp_forward(...); ''' # Load inline ext = load_inline( name='swin_mlp_cuda', cpp_sources=[cpp_source], cuda_sources=[cuda_source], functions=['spatial_mlp_forward', 'mlp_forward'], extra_cuda_cflags=['-O3'], verbose=True, ) class ModelNew(Model): def __init__(self, *args, **kwargs): super().__init__(*args, **kwargs) # ... any setup ... def forward(self, x): x = self.patch_embed(x) x = self.pos_drop(x) for layer in self.layers: for blk in layer.blocks: H, W = blk.input_resolution B, L, C = x.shape shortcut = x # Call fused spatial mlp x = x.view(B, H, W, C) x = spatial_mlp_forward( x, blk.norm1.weight, blk.norm1.bias, blk.spatial_mlp.weight, blk.spatial_mlp.bias, H, W, C, blk.num_heads, blk.window_size, blk.shift_size, blk.padding ) x = x.view(B, H*W, C) # Residual x = shortcut + x # Call fused MLP x = mlp_forward( x, blk.norm2.weight, blk.norm2.bias, blk.mlp.fc1.weight, blk.mlp.fc1.bias, blk.mlp.fc2.weight, blk.mlp.fc2.bias, C, int(C * blk.mlp_ratio) ) if layer.downsample is not None: x = layer.downsample(x) x = self.norm(x) x = self.avgpool(x.transpose(1, 2)) x = torch.flatten(x, 1) x = self.head(x) return x ``` But wait, `Model` is not available in the module scope. The setup code is executed with `exec()`, so the reference `Model` and other classes ARE in scope. Let me make `ModelNew` inherit from `Model` and just override `forward`. For the MLP, I need to handle `nn.Linear` weights. The weight shape is (out_features, in_features). I'll pass them to the CUDA kernel. For the spatial_mlp, `nn.Conv1d` weight shape is (out_channels, in_channels/groups, kernel_size) = (nH*ws*ws, ws*ws, 1). I'll reshape it in the Python wrapper before passing to CUDA. Actually, in the CUDA kernel for spatial_mlp, it's easier if the weight is already shaped as (num_heads, ws*ws, ws*ws). I'll do the reshape in Python. OK, let me now write the actual CUDA code. ### MLP Kernel CUDA Code ```cpp __device__ float gelu(float x) { return 0.5f * x * (1.0f + erff(x * 0.7071067811865476f)); } __global__ void mlp_kernel( const float* __restrict__ input, float* __restrict__ output, const float* __restrict__ norm_w, const float* __restrict__ norm_b, const float* __restrict__ fc1_w, const float* __restrict__ fc1_b, const float* __restrict__ fc2_w, const float* __restrict__ fc2_b, int N, int C, int H ) { int row = blockIdx.x; if (row >= N) return; extern __shared__ float smem[]; float* smem_input = smem; float* smem_inter = smem + C; float* smem_reduce = smem_inter + max(C, H); // Actually we don't need this much const float* input_row = input + row * C; // Load input for (int i = threadIdx.x; i < C; i += blockDim.x) { smem_input[i] = input_row[i]; } __syncthreads(); // LayerNorm float sum = 0.0f; for (int i = threadIdx.x; i < C; i += blockDim.x) { sum += smem_input[i]; } // Block reduction for sum for (int offset = blockDim.x / 2; offset > 0; offset /= 2) { sum += __shfl_down_sync(0xffffffff, sum, offset); } __shared__ float mean; if (threadIdx.x == 0) { mean = sum / C; } __syncthreads(); float sq_sum = 0.0f; for (int i = threadIdx.x; i < C; i += blockDim.x) { float diff = smem_input[i] - mean; sq_sum += diff * diff; } for (int offset = blockDim.x / 2; offset > 0; offset /= 2) { sq_sum += __shfl_down_sync(0xffffffff, sq_sum, offset); } __shared__ float inv_std; if (threadIdx.x == 0) { inv_std = rsqrtf(sq_sum / C + 1e-5f); } __syncthreads(); for (int i = threadIdx.x; i < C; i += blockDim.x) { smem_input[i] = (smem_input[i] - mean) * inv_std * norm_w[i] + norm_b[i]; } __syncthreads(); // FC1: C -> H for (int out_idx = threadIdx.x; out_idx < H; out_idx += blockDim.x) { float acc = fc1_b[out_idx]; for (int c = 0; c < C; c++) { acc += smem_input[c] * fc1_w[out_idx * C + c]; } smem_inter[out_idx] = acc; } __syncthreads(); // GELU for (int i = threadIdx.x; i < H; i += blockDim.x) { smem_inter[i] = gelu(smem_inter[i]); } __syncthreads(); // FC2: H -> C for (int out_idx = threadIdx.x; out_idx < C; out_idx += blockDim.x) { float acc = fc2_b[out_idx]; for (int h = 0; h < H; h++) { acc += smem_inter[h] * fc2_w[out_idx * H + h]; } acc += input_row[out_idx]; // residual output[row * C + out_idx] = acc; } } ``` Wait, I used `__shfl_down_sync` for reduction. This only works if blockDim.x is a warp size multiple and all threads participate. For blockDim=C=96, 96=3×32. `__shfl_down_sync` works within a warp, not across warps. For a block of 96 threads, `__shfl_down_sync` only reduces within each warp of 32 threads. I need to also reduce across warps. I should write a proper block reduction or use shared memory. Let me write a helper function: ```cpp __device__ float block_reduce_sum(float val) { int tid = threadIdx.x; int lane = tid % 32; int warp_id = tid / 32; // Warp reduction for (int offset = 16; offset > 0; offset /= 2) { val += __shfl_down_sync(0xffffffff, val, offset); } // Store warp sum to shared memory __shared__ float warp_sums[32]; // max 32 warps per block if (lane == 0) { warp_sums[warp_id] = val; } __syncthreads(); // Final reduction by first warp if (tid < 32) { val = (tid < blockDim.x / 32) ? warp_sums[tid] : 0.0f; for (int offset = 16; offset > 0; offset /= 2) { val += __shfl_down_sync(0xffffffff, val, offset); } } return val; } ``` This handles up to 1024 threads (32 warps). But wait, for blockDim.x = 96, there are 3 warps. The function works: - `lane == 0` stores warp sum to `warp_sums[0]`, `warp_sums[1]`, `warp_sums[2]`. - Then first warp loads `warp_sums[0]`, `warp_sums[1]`, `warp_sums[2]`. Threads 3-31 load 0. - Reduces to a single value in thread 0. But then how do all threads get the final value? In my function, only threads with tid < 32 get the result. I need to broadcast it back. ```cpp __device__ float block_reduce_sum(float val) { __shared__ float warp_sums[32]; __shared__ float result; int tid = threadIdx.x; int lane = tid % 32; int warp_id = tid / 32; // Warp reduction for (int offset = 16; offset > 0; offset /= 2) { val += __shfl_down_sync(0xffffffff, val, offset); } if (lane == 0) { warp_sums[warp_id] = val; } __syncthreads(); if (tid < 32) { val = (tid < (blockDim.x + 31) / 32) ? warp_sums[tid] : 0.0f; for (int offset = 16; offset > 0; offset /= 2) { val += __shfl_down_sync(0xffffffff, val, offset); } if (tid == 0) result = val; } __syncthreads(); return result; } ``` Then in the kernel: ```cpp float sum = ...; sum = block_reduce_sum(sum); float mean = sum / C; ``` But `block_reduce_sum` returns the same value to ALL threads because of the `__syncthreads()` and `result` in shared memory. Good. Now, about the `erff` function: is it available in CUDA? Yes, it's in `math.h` or CUDA's built-in. For device code, we might need `erff` from the CUDA math library. Actually, for fp32, CUDA provides `erff()`. Let me make sure to include `<cmath>` or just use it directly. Also, I should handle the shared memory size properly. For the MLP kernel: - smem_input: C floats - smem_inter: H floats - warp_sums: 32 floats - mean, inv_std: 2 floats - Total: C + H + 34 floats For C=768, H=3072: 768 + 3072 + 34 = 3874 floats = 15.5 KB. Well within shared memory limits. OK, for the MLP kernel launch: - blockDim.x = C (or next power of 2 if needed, but C divides nicely) - Actually, since we use `for (int i = threadIdx.x; i < C; i += blockDim.x)`, blockDim doesn't have to be C. But using blockDim=C avoids warp divergence. - Grid: (N, 1, 1) where N = B * H * W For the spatial_mlp kernel, I need two kernels: 1. `window_gather_mlp`: Computes the grouped matmul on windows 2. `window_scatter`: Scatters results back to the image with unshift + residual Actually, let me think about whether I can simplify the spatial_mlp. The spatial_mlp path: ```python shortcut = x x = self.norm1(x) # (B, H*W, C) x = x.view(B, H, W, C) # shift and pad if shift_size > 0: P_l, P_r, P_t, P_b = self.padding shifted_x = F.pad(x, [0, 0, P_l, P_r, P_t, P_b], "constant", 0) else: shifted_x = x # partition windows x_windows = window_partition(shifted_x, self.window_size) x_windows = x_windows.view(-1, self.window_size * self.window_size, C) x_windows_heads = x_windows.view(-1, window_size*window_size, num_heads, C//num_heads).transpose(1, 2) x_windows_heads = x_windows_heads.reshape(-1, num_heads * window_size * window_size, C // num_heads) spatial_mlp_windows = self.spatial_mlp(x_windows_heads) spatial_mlp_windows = spatial_mlp_windows.view(-1, num_heads, window_size*window_size, C//num_heads).transpose(1, 2) spatial_mlp_windows = spatial_mlp_windows.reshape(-1, window_size * window_size, C) # merge windows shifted_x = window_reverse(spatial_mlp_windows, self.window_size, _H, _W) # reverse shift if self.shift_size > 0: x = shifted_x[:, P_t:-P_b, P_l:-P_r, :].contiguous() else: x = shifted_x x = x.view(B, H*W, C) x = shortcut + self.drop_path(x) ``` This is the first half (before the MLP). We want to replace ALL of this with custom kernels. Since PyTorch's Conv1d is efficient for the grouped conv, maybe I should keep it but eliminate the copies. I'll use `einsum` for the spatial_mlp part. New plan for spatial_mlp path: 1. Custom kernel `layernorm_fused`: x (B, H*W, C) → normalized x, compute in-place or output. Actually, we can do LayerNorm in PyTorch and it will be fast. 2. Custom kernel `window_partition_einsum`: - Takes padded image (B, _H, _W, C) - Gathers windows into tensor (nWB, num_heads, ws*ws, C/nH) - Wait, maybe I should do this in Python with custom reshape logic. Actually, here's a much simpler idea: PyTorch's `as_strided` can create the window_partition view without copying! Then we just need the right permutation. ```python # window_partition without copy: B, H, W, C = x.shape x = x.as_strided((B, H//ws, ws, W//ws, ws, C), (H*W*C, ws*W*C, W*C, ws*C, C, 1)) windows = x.permute(0, 1, 3, 2, 4, 5).reshape(-1, ws, ws, C) ``` But `.permute` followed by `.reshape` might not be contiguous, so a copy is needed for the conv to work. Unless... what if we express the conv1d as a `convolution` that can handle strided inputs? PyTorch's conv functions usually require contiguous inputs. I'll write: 1. `spatial_mlp_kernel`: does everything from padded input to window_mlp output (temporary) 2. `window_scatter_add_kernel`: takes shortcut, temp output, and writes shortcut + temp to final output For kernel 1, I'll implement the grouped matmul directly. Actually, looking at the whole code, I realize writing all this CUDA is very error-prone. Yes, correct. The CUDA kernel accesses it as `weight[(head * ws_sq + pos) * ws_sq + j]`. This is `(head, pos, j)` in row-major, which matches the reshaped tensor. Similarly, `conv_b` is reshaped to `(num_heads, ws*sq)`. Accessed as `bias[head * ws_sq + pos]`. Correct. One more thing: the `window_mlp_kernel` might have bank conflicts if `ch_per_head` is a multiple of 32. With `ch_per_head = 32`, and threads loading consecutive `idx = threadIdx.x`, the memory accesses are: - `smem_window[idx]` where idx = 0, 1, 2, ... - Shared memory is banked. For 32 banks and 32-bit floats, bank = (address / 4) % 32. - Thread 0 accesses bank 0, thread 1 accesses bank 1, etc. Fully coalesced shared memory access! No bank conflicts. Great. For the computation loop: - Thread idx accesses `smem_window[j * ch_per_head + ch]`. - With `ch_per_head = 32`, `j * 32 + ch`. - If consecutive threads have the same ch but different j? No, consecutive threads have consecutive idx, which means consecutive `ch` for the same `pos`, or moving to the next `pos`. - In the dot product, for a fixed `idx` (fixed `pos` and `ch`), as `j` varies: - `j * 32 + ch`. For j=0: bank = ch%32. For j=1: bank = (32+ch)%32 = ch%32. - All threads with the same `ch` but different `pos` access the SAME bank but different addresses. Wait, for a fixed `j`, consecutive threads with consecutive `idx` might have the same `ch` modulo 32? Let's see. Actually, threads in the loop access based on `idx`, not threadIdx.x. Each thread computes its own `idx`, then in the inner loop iterates `j` from 0 to 48. For each `j`, the thread loads `smem_window[j * 32 + ch]`. Since `ch` is fixed for a given thread, and `j` varies, the accessed addresses differ by multiples of 32 floats = 128 bytes. Each bank is 4 bytes. So consecutive j values access the same bank but spaced 128 bytes apart. No conflict if each thread accesses one at a time. But what if consecutive threads have the same `ch`? They would access the same bank at the same time, causing conflicts. Consecutive `idx` values: idx=0 → (pos=0, ch=0), idx=1 → (pos=0, ch=1), ..., idx=31 → (pos=0, ch=31), idx=32 → (pos=1, ch=0). So threads 0-31 have ch=0..31. Each different! Great. For j fixed, thread 0 accesses bank (j*32+0)%32 = 0, thread 1 accesses bank 1, etc. All different banks! Perfect. For j varying, all threads still access different banks for a fixed j. Excellent. This kernel should have no shared memory bank conflicts. Now, for launching, `threads = 256` for `window_mlp_kernel`. `ws_sq * ch_per_head = 49 * 32 = 1568`. With 256 threads, each thread handles `1568 / 256 = 6.125` elements. Not integer, so some threads do 6, some do 7, via the loop `for (int idx = threadIdx.x; idx < ws_sq * ch_per_head; idx += blockDim.x)`. This has no divergence. OK, I think the code is mostly correct. Let me write it more carefully and compile it. One more issue: `__shfl_down_sync` requires compute capability >= 3.0, which is fine for H100. But I should make sure to use the correct mask. Actually, for `warp_reduce_sum`, doing `for (int offset = 16; offset > 0; offset /= 2)` with full mask `0xffffffff` is correct only if all 32 threads in the warp participate. This is true because `blockDim.x` is at least 32 (in our case C=96 or more). For the last warp, if blockDim.x is not a multiple of 32, some threads might be inactive. But our block sizes are 96, 192, 384, 768, which are all multiples of 32. So it's fine. Wait, `blockDim.x` for `mlp_block_kernel` is C, which is 96, 192, 384, or 768. All multiples of 32. Good. For `window_mlp_kernel`, blockDim.x = 256, which is a multiple of 32. Good. For `window_scatter_kernel`, blockDim.x = 256. Good. Let me now write the clean version and try to compile.

turn 3llm latency: 26.5s
reasoning
The issue is that I'm mixing CUDA code in cpp_sources. For load_inline, I need to separate the CUDA kernels (with __global__ etc.) into cuda_sources, and only have pure C++ CPU-side code in cpp_sources (with only torch binding code). Let me fix this by putting all kernels in cuda_sources and only declaring/wrapping in cpp_sources. Actually, looking at the compile output, both main.cpp (generated from cpp_sources) and cuda.cu (generated from cuda_sources) are being compiled. The problem is that I have CUDA-specific code (__syncthreads, blockIdx, etc.) in cpp_sources which should only be in the .cu file. Let me make cpp_sources only contain the C++ host code and cuda_sources only contain kernels.
call → compile_kernel

offset = 16; offset > 0; offset /= 2) {\n val += __shfl_down_sync(0xffffffff, val, offset);\n }\n return val;\n}\n\n__device__ float block_reduce_sum(float val) {\n __shared__ float warp_sums[32];\n static __shared__ float result;\n \n int lane = threadIdx.x % 32;\n int warp_id = threadIdx.x / 32;\n \n val = warp_reduce_sum(val);\n \n if (lane == 0) {\n warp_sums[warp_id] = val;\n }\n __syncthreads();\n \n if (threadIdx.x < 32) {\n val = (threadIdx.x < (blockDim.x + 31) / 32) ? warp_sums[threadIdx.x] : 0.0f;\n val = warp_reduce_sum(val);\n if (threadIdx.x == 0) result = val;\n }\n __syncthreads();\n return result;\n}\n\n__global__ void mlp_block_kernel(\n const float* __restrict__ input,\n float* __restrict__ output,\n const float* __restrict__ norm_w,\n const float* __restrict__ norm_b,\n const float* __restrict__ fc1_w,\n const float* __restrict__ fc1_b,\n const float* __restrict__ fc2_w,\n const float* __restrict__ fc2_b,\n int N, int C, int H\n) {\n int row = blockIdx.x;\n if (row >= N) return;\n \n extern __shared__ float smem[];\n float* smem_input = smem;\n float* smem_inter = smem + C;\n \n const float* input_row = input + row * C;\n \n for (int i = threadIdx.x; i < C; i += blockDim.x) {\n smem_input[i] = input_row[i];\n }\n __syncthreads();\n \n float sum = 0.0f;\n for (int i = threadIdx.x; i < C; i += blockDim.x) {\n sum += smem_input[i];\n }\n float mean = block_reduce_sum(sum) / C;\n __syncthreads();\n \n float sq_sum = 0.0f;\n for (int i = threadIdx.x; i < C; i += blockDim.x) {\n float diff = smem_input[i] - mean;\n sq_sum += diff * diff;\n }\n float var = block_reduce_sum(sq_sum) / C;\n __syncthreads();\n \n float inv_std = rsqrtf(var + 1e-5f);\n for (int i = threadIdx.x; i < C; i += blockDim.x) {\n smem_input[i] = (smem_input[i] - mean) * inv_std * norm_w[i] + norm_b[i];\n }\n __syncthreads();\n \n for (int out_idx = threadIdx.x; out_idx < H; out_idx += blockDim.x) {\n float acc = fc1_b[out_idx];\n for (int c = 0; c < C; c++) {\n acc += smem_input[c] * fc1_w[out_idx * C + c];\n }\n smem_inter[out_idx] = acc;\n }\n __syncthreads();\n \n for (int i = threadIdx.x; i < H; i += blockDim.x) {\n smem_inter[i] = gelu_kernel(smem_inter[i]);\n }\n __syncthreads();\n \n for (int out_idx = threadIdx.x; out_idx < C; out_idx += blockDim.x) {\n float acc = fc2_b[out_idx];\n for (int h = 0; h < H; h++) {\n acc += smem_inter[h] * fc2_w[out_idx * H + h];\n }\n acc += input_row[out_idx];\n output[row * C + out_idx] = acc;\n }\n}\n\n__global__ void window_mlp_kernel(\n const float* __restrict__ padded_input,\n float* __restrict__ window_output,\n const float* __restrict__ weight,\n const float* __restrict__ bias,\n int B, int _H, int _W, int C, int num_heads, int ws\n) {\n int nwb = blockIdx.x;\n int head = blockIdx.y;\n int windows_per_row = _W / ws;\n int total_windows_h = _H / ws;\n int total_windows = B * total_windows_h * windows_per_row;\n if (nwb >= total_windows || head >= num_heads) return;\n \n int b = nwb / (total_windows_h * windows_per_row);\n int win_rem = nwb % (total_windows_h * windows_per_row);\n int wh = win_rem / windows_per_row;\n int ww = win_rem % windows_per_row;\n \n int ch_per_head = C / num_heads;\n int ws_sq = ws * ws;\n \n extern __shared__ float smem[];\n float* smem_window = smem;\n \n for (int idx = threadIdx.x; idx < ws_sq * ch_per_head; idx += blockDim.x) {\n turn 4llm latency: 19.5s
reasoning
I see the fundamental issue - we can't use `load_inline` with both `cpp_sources` and `cuda_sources` as two separate strings when the functions have dependencies between them. The CUDA kernels are not being declared in the scope of the C++ wrapper. They only get compiled as `.cu` file, and the CUDA driver API might not be able to resolve them properly when called from the `.cpp` file. The proper way is to put everything in a single `.cu` file using `load_inline` with only `cuda_sources`. Let me rewrite it. Also, I noticed some issues in the forward method - I'm using `self.norm1` instead of `blk.norm1`. Let me create a much simpler approach. Looking at the code again, I think the best approach is: 1. Create one `.cu` file with everything 2. Use only `cuda_sources=` parameter in `load_inline` 3. Also I need to be more careful about the forward call - use `blk.norm1`, not `self.norm1` Let me rewrite carefully.

turn 6llm latency: 19.9s
reasoning
The issue is that `cpp_sources` is being compiled as a C++ file (main.cpp) and the CUDA code inside it causes errors because CUDA intrinsics like `__shfl_down_sync`, `threadIdx`, etc., are not available in C++ code. The C++ source should only contain host-side wrapper functions, while the CUDA source should only contain the kernels. Let me fix this by having a proper separation: - `cpp_sources`: only C++ host-side wrapper functions (with the CUDA kernels as `extern void` declarations) - `cuda_sources`: only the CUDA kernels (device functions and `__global__` kernels) Actually, looking at `load_inline`'s implementation, when both `cpp_sources` and `cuda_sources` are provided, the `cpp_sources` gets compiled as C++ and `cuda_sources` as CUDA. The C++ file just needs declarations of the kernels and then the host wrapper functions that launch them. The CUDA file contains the actual kernel implementations. Let me create this proper separation.
call → compile_kernel
callcompile_kernel { "kernel_code": "\nimport torch\nimport torch.nn as nn\nfrom torch.utils.cpp_extension import load_inline\n\ncuda_source = r'''\n#include <torch/extension.h>\n#include offset = 16; offset > 0; offset /= 2) {\n val += __shfl_down_sync(0xffffffff, val, offset);\n }\n return val;\n}\n\n__device__ float block_reduce_sum(float val) {\n __shared__ float warp_sums[32];\n static __shared__ float result;\n int lane = threadIdx.x % 32;\n int warp_id = threadIdx.x / 32;\n val = warp_reduce_sum(val);\n if (lane == 0) {\n warp_sums[warp_id] = val;\n }\n __syncthreads();\n if (threadIdx.x < 32) {\n val = (threadIdx.x < (blockDim.x + 31) / 32) ? warp_sums[threadIdx.x] : 0.0f;\n val = warp_reduce_sum(val);\n if (threadIdx.x == 0) result = val;\n }\n __syncthreads();\n return result;\n}\n\n__global__ void mlp_block_kernel(\n const float* __restrict__ input,\n float* __restrict__ output,\n const float* __restrict__ norm_w,\n const float* __restrict__ norm_b,\n const float* __restrict__ fc1_w,\n const float* __restrict__ fc1_b,\n const float* __restrict__ fc2_w,\n const float* __restrict__ fc2_b,\n int N, int C, int H\n) {\n int row = blockIdx.x;\n if (row >= N) return;\n extern __shared__ float smem[];\n float* smem_input = smem;\n float* smem_inter = smem + C;\n const float* input_row = input + row * C;\n for (int i = threadIdx.x; i < C; i += blockDim.x) {\n smem_input[i] = input_row[i];\n }\n __syncthreads();\n float sum = 0.0f;\n for (int i = threadIdx.x; i < C; i += blockDim.x) {\n sum += smem_input[i];\n }\n float mean = block_reduce_sum(sum) / C;\n __syncthreads();\n float sq_sum = 0.0f;\n for (int i = threadIdx.x; i < C; i += blockDim.x) {\n float diff = smem_input[i] - mean;\n sq_sum += diff * diff;\n }\n float var = block_reduce_sum(sq_sum) / C;\n __syncthreads();\n float inv_std = rsqrtf(var + 1e-5f);\n for (int i = threadIdx.x; i < C; i += blockDim.x) {\n smem_input[i] = (smem_input[i] - mean) * inv_std * norm_w[i] + norm_b[i];\n }\n __syncthreads();\n for (int out_idx = threadIdx.x; out_idx < H; out_idx += blockDim.x) {\n float acc = fc1_b[out_idx];\n for (int c = 0; c < C; c++) {\n acc += smem_input[c] * fc1_w[out_idx * C + c];\n }\n smem_inter[out_idx] = acc;\n }\n __syncthreads();\n for (int i = threadIdx.x; i < H; i += blockDim.x) {\n smem_inter[i] = gelu_kernel(smem_inter[i]);\n }\n __syncthreads();\n for (int out_idx = threadIdx.x; out_idx < C; out_idx += blockDim.x) {\n float acc = fc2_b[out_idx];\n for (int h = 0; h < H; h++) {\n acc += smem_inter[h] * fc2_w[out_idx * H + h];\n }\n acc += input_row[out_idx];\n output[row * C + out_idx] = acc;\n }\n}\n\n__global__ void window_mlp_kernel(\n const float* __restrict__ padded_input,\n float* __restrict__ window_output,\n const float* __restrict__ weight,\n const float* __restrict__ bias,\n int B, int _H, int _W, int C, int num_heads, int ws\n) {\n int nwb = blockIdx.x;\n int head = blockIdx.y;\n int windows_per_row = _W / ws;\n int total_windows_h = _H / ws;\n int total_windows = B * total_windows_h * windows_per_row;\n if (nwb >= total_windows || head >= num_heads) return;\n int b = nwb / (total_windows_h * windows_per_row);\n int win_rem = nwb % (total_windows_h * windows_per_row);\n int wh = win_rem / windows_per_row;\n int ww = win_rem % windows_per_row;\n int ch_per_head = C / num_heads;\n int ws_sq = ws * ws;\n extern __shared__ float smem[];\n float* smem_window = smem;\n for (int idx = threadIdx.x; idx < ws_sq * ch_per_head; idx += blockDim.x) {\n int pos = idx / ch_per_head;\n int ch = idx % ch_per_head;\n int ih = pos / ws;\n int iw = pos % ws;\n int h = wh * ws + ih;\n int w = ww * ws + iw;\n int input_idx = ((b * _H + h) * _W + w) * C + head * ch_per_head + ch;\n smem_window[idx] = padded_input[input_idx];\n }\n __syncthreads();\n for (int idx = threadIdx.x; idx < ws_sq * ch_per_head; idx += blockDim.x) {\n int pos = idx / ch_per_head;\n int ch = idx % ch_per_head;\n float acc = bias[head * ws_sq + pos];\n for (int j = 0; j < ws_sq; j++) {\n acc += smem_window[j * ch_per_head + ch] * \n weight[(head * ws_sq + pos) * ws_sq + j];\n }\n window_output[((nwb * num_heads + head) * ws_sq + pos) * ch_per_head + ch] = acc;\n }\n}\n\n__global__ void window_scatter_kernel(\n const float* __restrict__ window_input,\n float* __restrict__ output,\n int B, int _H, int _W, int H_orig, threadIdx.x;\n if (idx >= total_elements) return;\n int c = idx % C;\n int w = (idx / C) % W_orig;\n int h = (idx / (C * W_orig)) % H_orig;\n int b = idx / (C * W_orig * H_orig);\n int hp = h + P_t;\n int wp = w + P_l;\n int wh = hp / ws;\n int ww = wp / ws;\n int ih = hp % ws;\n int iw = wp % ws;\n int pos = ih * ws + iw;\n int windows_per_row = _W / ws;\n int nwb = b * (_H / ws) * windows_per_row + wh * windows_per_row + ww;\n int head = c / (C / num_heads);\n int ch = c % (C / num_heads);\n int ws_sq = ws * ws;\n int input_idx = ((nwb * num_heads + head) * ws_sq + pos) * (C / num_heads) + ch;\n output[idx] = window_input[input_idx];\n}\n'''\n\ncpp_source = r'''\n#include <torch/extension.h>\n\nvoid mlp_block_kernel(\n const float*, float*,\n const float*, const float*,\n const float*, = torch::empty_like(input);\n int threads = std::min(C, 256);\n int shared_mem = (C + H) * sizeof(float) + 256;\n mlp_block_kernel<<<N, threads, shared_mem>>>(\n input.data_ptr<float>(),\n H\n );\n return output;\n}\n\ntorch::Tensor spatial_mlp_forward(\n torch::Tensor x,\n torch::Tensor conv_w,\n torch::Tensor conv_b,\n int H, int W, int C, int num_heads, int ws, int shift_size,\n int P_l, int P_r, int P_t, int P_b\n) {\n int B = x.size(0);\n torch::Tensor padded;\n int _H = H, _W = W;\n if (shift_size > 0) {\n padded = torch::constant_pad_nd(x, {0, 0, P_l, P_r, P_t, P_b}, 0);\n _H = H + P_t + P_b;\n _W = W + P_l + P_r;\n } else {\n padded = x;\n }\n int ws_sq = ws * ws;\n int total_windows = B * (_H / ws) * (_W / ws);\n auto window_output = torch::zeros({total_windows, num_heads, ws_sq, C / num_heads}, x.options());\n dim3 grid(total_windows, num_heads);\n int threads = 256;\n int shared = ws_sq * (C / num_heads) * sizeof(float);\n window_mlp_kernel<<<grid, threads, reasoning
The issue is that nvcc needs to compile the CUDA kernels, and the cpp_sources should only contain the host wrapper code that calls the kernels. The problem is that the cpp_source (which becomes main.cpp) doesn't have access to the __global__ function declarations when using the <<< >>> syntax. Let me try a different approach: put everything in the cuda_sources only, and use an empty or minimal cpp_sources. I need to use torch.utils.cpp_extension.load_inline properly, where cpp_sources is just the wrapper signatures/declarations and cuda_sources contains the actual kernel implementations. Actually, let me look at how load_inline works more carefully. When we pass both cpp_sources and cuda_sources, the cpp_sources gets compiled as C++ (main.cpp) and cuda_sources gets compiled as CUDA (cuda.cu). The issue is that the C++ compiler doesn't understand <<< >>> syntax. I think the proper way is: - cpp_sources: only the wrapper function declarations (no kernel launches) - cuda_sources: both the kernels and the wrapper functions that launch them Actually, I think the most reliable approach is to put the wrapper functions (that call kernels via <<< >>>) in the cuda_sources file, and just have extern declarations in cpp_sources, or put everything in cuda_sources. Let me try with cpp_sources being empty or just forward declarations, and everything else in cuda_sources.
